Question 143 (GMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
A 5 years old child presented to ENT OPD with complete bilateral nasal obstruction and snoring for the last 2 years. Local examination shows smooth rounded swelling of the septum in both nasal cavities. On palpation mass fluctuant and soft. According to the parents the child had a history of fall few days back. A diagnosis of septal abscess was made. Which type of specific nasal deformity can occur if the treatment is delayed?

Options:
  Columella retraction
  Deviated nasal septum
  Nasal ala retraction
✓ Supratip depression
  Septal perforation

Explanation:
A septal abscess destroys the septal cartilage due to avascular necrosis. The loss of cartilaginous support leads to the collapse of the nasal dorsum, resulting in a supratip depression or 'saddle nose' deformity.

Question 144 (GMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
A new born baby shifted to ENT ward from NURSERY unit having history of severe bilateral nasal obstruction, feeding difficulty and cyclical cyanosis that improves while crying and worsens with feeding. What is most probable diagnosis?

Options:
✓ Choanal atresia
  Congenital vocal cord paralysis
  Laryngomalacia
  Laryngeal web
  Meconium aspiration

Explanation:
Cyclical cyanosis that improves with crying (when the baby breathes through the mouth) and worsens with feeding (when the mouth is closed) is the hallmark of bilateral choanal atresia in a newborn.

================================================================================
Question 145 (GMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
A 10 years old boy presented with unilateral nasal obstruction on the left side for the last 10 month s. Local examination shows a smooth rounded and a grayish mass which does not bleed on palpation. What is the most probable diagnosis?

Options:
  Allergic nasal polyps
  Angiofibroma
✓ Antrochoanal polyp
  Enlarged turbinate
  Rhinolith

Explanation:
A unilateral, smooth, grayish, non-bleeding nasal mass in a child is most likely an antrochoanal polyp. Angiofibromas are red and bleed easily. Allergic polyps are usually bilateral.

Question 146 (GMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
A 20 years old man presented with severe pain over the tip of the nose. On examination tip of the nose is swollen, reddish in color and severely tender. According to the patient he had the same problem 2 months back after visiting a barber shop. What is the most probable diagnosis?

Options:
  Acute sinusitis
  Allergic rhinitis
✓ Nasal vestibulitis
  Rhinitis sicca
  Septal abscess

Explanation:
Localized pain, redness, swelling, and tenderness at the nasal tip (vestibule), often associated with hair follicle infection (furunculosis) after trauma (like trimming nose hairs), is diagnostic of nasal vestibulitis.

Question 147 (GMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
A 4 years old girl brought to ENT OPD with history of on off bleeding from left side of the nose. His mother so complains of foul smell from the nose for the last 3 weeks. Local examination shows a rounded swelling covered by bloody stained greenish secretions. What is the most probable diagnosis?

  Atrophic rhinitis
  Fungal sinusitis

  Rhinitis sicca

Explanation:
Unilateral foul-smelling nasal discharge, often bloody, in a young child is the classic presentation of a retained nasal foreign body.

Question 148 (GMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
A 25 years old male presented to ENT OPD with history of recurrent bleeding from the nose on left side for the last few months. He also further narrated that he also appreciates a foul smell from the nose on the same side. Local examination of the nose shows a rounded blackish swelling covered by mucus. Palpation of the swelling reveals a cracking sound. What is the most probable diagnosis?

Options:
  Allergic nasal polyp
  Antro-choanal polyp
  Nasopharyngeal carcinoma
  Foreign body nose
✓ Rhinolith

Explanation:
A unilateral, foul-smelling nasal mass that is hard, blackish (due to oxidation), and produces a 'cracking' or stony sensation on probing is characteristic of a rhinolith (stone formation around a nidus).

Question 149 (GMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
A 50 yrs old female presented to ENT OPD with nasal obstruction, foul smell from the nose for the last 2 years. On examination the inferior turbinates are shrunken and the nose is roomy and full of greenish crusts. She further narrated that few years back she had some sort of nasal surgery from the local ENT specialist. What is your most probable diagnose?

✓ Atrophic rhinitis
  Allergic nasal polyp
  Rhinitis sicca
  Rhinitis caseosa
  Rhinolith

Explanation:
The clinical findings of roomy nose, crusting, foul smell, and turbinate atrophy, especially following nasal surgery (secondary atrophic rhinitis, likely due to excessive turbinate resection), confirm the diagnosis of Atrophic Rhinitis.

Question 150 (GMC - 2024)

Question:
A Young boy of seventeen Years presented with nasal obstruction, recurrent, profuse epistaxis on left side of the nose for the last 6 months.   Nasopharyngeal carcinoma

Question 151 (GMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
During tracheostomy surgeon usually avoids incision at first tracheal ring to prevent post-operative complication of:

Options:
  Aspiration
  Hematoma
  Pneumonia
✓ Subglottic stenosis
  Vocal cord paralysis

Explanation:
Incising the first tracheal ring can damage the cricoid cartilage or cause inflammation in the subglottic region, leading to the serious complication of subglottic stenosis.

================================================================================
Question 152 (GMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
During post-operative care of tracheostomy in first 2 days the cuff of tracheostomy tube is deflated for few minutes after every few hours to avoid:

Options:
  Aspiration
  Hematoma
✓ Ischemic necrosis of trachea
  Subglottic stenosis
  Vocal cord paralysis

Explanation:
Continuous pressure from the tracheostomy cuff on the tracheal wall can exceed capillary perfusion pressure, leading to ischemic necrosis. Periodic deflation helps prevent this.

================================================================================
Question 153 (GMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
A middle-aged male comes to the OPD with the only complaint of hoarseness for the last 2 years. He has been a chronic smoker for 30 years. On examination, a reddish area of mucosal irregularity overlying a portion of his cords was seen. Management would include all except:

Options:
  General endoscopy
  Cessation of smoking
  Micro laryngeal surgery for biopsy
  Regular follow-up
✓ Tracheostomy

Explanation:
For a suspicious laryngeal lesion in a smoker, biopsy and evaluation are crucial. Tracheostomy is indicated only for airway obstruction, which is not mentioned here; therefore, it is not part of the immediate management plan.

================================================================================
Question 154 (GMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
A case of carcinoma larynx with the involvement of anterior commisure and right vocal cord developed perichondritis of thyroid cartilage. Which one of the following statement is true for the management of this case?

Options:
  He should first receive radiotherapy and if residual tumor is present then should undergo laryngectomy
  He should be given chemotherapy and then radiotherapy
  He should be given radical radiotherapy as this can cure early tumors
  He should be treated with combination of chemotherapy and radiotherapy
✓ He should first undergo laryngectomy and then postoperative radiotherapy

Explanation:
Involvement of the thyroid cartilage (perichondritis/invasion) typically upstages laryngeal cancer to T4. Radiotherapy is generally less effective when there is cartilage involvement or perichondritis. Total laryngectomy followed by postoperative radiotherapy is the standard curative approach for such advanced disease.

================================================================================
Question 155 (GMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
A 20-year-old male presents with throat pain and easy fatigability of his voice. Indirect laryngeal examination revealed both cords approximately well but leaving a triangular gap in the inter-arytenoid. He getting relief from treatment. Your diagnosis is:

Options:
  Functional aphonia
  Mogiphonia
  Mutational falsetto voice
✓ Phonousthenia
  Ventricular dysphonia

Explanation:
The symptoms of voice fatigue (phonasthenia) and throat pain, along with the finding of a posterior glottic chink (triangular gap) due to weak adduction (often associated with muscle tension dysphonia or weakness), are consistent with Phonasthenia.

================================================================================
Question 156 (GMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
A 4-year-old male child presents with multiple laryngeal papillomas with hoarse voice and slight airway distress. Papillomas are involving the glottis. The best treatment is:

Options:
  Examination under 0/L
  Antibiotics & IV fluid
✓ Microlaryngoscopy and excision
  Steroids
  Tracheostomy and observation

Explanation:
The treatment of choice for Juvenile Laryngeal Papillomatosis is surgical removal to maintain the airway. Microlaryngoscopy with excision (e.g., CO2 laser or microdebrider) is the standard method.

================================================================================
Question 157 (GMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
The preferred surgical procedure for prevention of aspiration is:

Options:
  Feeding gastrostomy/jejunostomy
  Nasogastric tube
  Thyroplasty
✓ Tracheal division and permanent tracheostom
  Cricothyrodotomy

Explanation:
For intractable aspiration, separation of the airway from the digestive tract is required. Laryngotracheal separation (tracheal division) with permanent tracheostomy is a definitive surgical method to prevent aspiration.

================================================================================
Question 158 (GMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Vocal cords nodules arise from which part of the larynx?

Options:
  Anterior commissure
  Anterior 1/3 rd of vocal cords
✓ At the junction of anterior 1/3 rd and posterior 2/3rd of vocal cords
  At the junction of anterior 1/3 rd and posterior 2/3rd of vocal cords
  Posterior 1/3rd of vocal cords

Explanation:
Vocal nodules characteristically develop at the junction of the anterior one-third and posterior two-thirds of the vocal cords, which is the point of maximal vibration and impact.

================================================================================
Question 159 (GMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
A 50-year-old man presents with a mass of lymph nodes in the upper cervical region. Complete physical examination of the upper aerodigestive tract did not reveal any primary tumor. He is a smoker and also drinks three times per week. Our next diagnostic step should be:

Options:
  CT scan neck
  Excision Biopsy of cervical nodes
✓ Fine needle aspiration cytology (FNAC) of the mass
  Lymph nodes
  Incisional biopsy of node of lymph nodes

Explanation:
In an adult with a neck mass, especially with risk factors, metastatic malignancy must be ruled out. FNAC is the standard initial diagnostic procedure. Open biopsy is contraindicated as it can disrupt tissue planes and compromise future neck dissection.

Question 160 (GMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
A parapharyngeal mass displacing the tonsil and tonsillar fossa medially with pulsations on Intralpation is mainly due to:

Options:
  Carotid body tumor
  Hodgkin's lymphoma
✓ Internal carotid artery aneurysm
  Non-Hodgkin's lymphoma
  Schwannoma of parapharyngeal space

Explanation:
A pulsatile mass in the parapharyngeal space displacing the tonsil medially is strongly suggestive of a vascular lesion, such as an internal carotid artery aneurysm or a highly vascular tumor like a glomus tumor.

Question 161 (GMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
A 30-year-old male presented with trismus, fever, swelling pushing the tonsils medially and spreading laterally to the neck posterior to sternocleidomastoid muscle. He gives a history of extraction of third molar 3 days back for dental caries. The diagnosis is:

Options:
  Ludwig's angina
✓ Parapharyngeal abscess
  Peritonsillar abscess
  Retropharyngeal abscess
  Submental abscess

Explanation:
Infection from a third molar can spread to the parapharyngeal space. The clinical signs of trismus, medial displacement of the tonsil, and lateral neck swelling are classic for a parapharyngeal abscess.

Most common type of esophageal cancer is:

Options:
  Adenocarcinoma
  Adenoid cystic carcinoma
  Mucoepidermoid carcinoma
✓ Squamous cell carcinoma
  Zenker diverticulum

Question 163 (GMC - 2024)

  Gross opacity covering mediastinum
  Diffuse esophageal spasms
  Esophageal dilatation
  Failure of lower esophageal sphincter to relax
✓ Rat-tail appearance

Achalasia is characterized by a 'bird-beak' appearance on barium swallow due to tapering. 'Rat-tail' appearance is typically associated with esophageal carcinoma.

Question 164 (GMC - 2024)

  Trigeminal neuralgia

Explanation:
Glossopharyngeal neuralgia causes severe pain in the throat/tonsillar fossa, radiating to the ear and posterior tongue, often triggered by swallowing.

Question 165 (GMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Elective neck dissection pertains to:

Options:
  Radical neck dissection with preservation of spinal accessory nerve
  Radical neck dissection for N neck
  Radical neck dissection where nodes are palpable
  Radical neck dissection with preservation of internal jugular vein
✓ Removal of some lymph node groups while sparing the others

Explanation:
Elective neck dissection (usually a selective neck dissection) involves removing lymph nodes from the neck in a patient with no clinical evidence of metastasis (N0 neck) to treat occult disease. It typically spares non-lymphatic structures and some node levels.

================================================================================
Question 166 (GMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Which of the following structures are preserved in a radical neck dissection?

Options:
  Hypoglossal, vagus and spinal accessory
  External jugular vein and external laryngeal nerve
  Sternocleidomastoid & glossopharyngeal nerve
✓ Sternocleidomastoid, spinal accessory and vagus nerve

Explanation:
A classic radical neck dissection removes the SCM, IJV, and spinal accessory nerve. However, the question asks what is *preserved*. The vagus nerve is preserved. The options are confusing or contain errors (e.g., option d lists SCM and accessory nerve which are *removed*). Assuming the question implies a *modified* radical or is testing knowledge of what is *not* taken, Vagus is the key structure preserved. But standard RND sacrifices XI. The vagus is preserved.

================================================================================
Question 167 (GMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Extranodal lymphoma is commonly a manifestation of:

Options:
  Hodgkin's disease
  Tuberculosis
  Both (a) and (b)
✓ Burkitt's lymphoma
  Squamous cell carcinoma

Explanation:
Non-Hodgkin lymphomas, such as Burkitt's lymphoma, frequently present with extranodal involvement (e.g., jaw, abdomen), unlike Hodgkin's disease which is typically nodal.

Question 168 (GMC - 2024)

✓ Arises from remnants of second branchial cleft
  Cyst may contain thyroid tissue
  Excision of body of Hyoid bone is necessary to prevent recurrence
  It is mobile
  Presents as a midline swelling near the hyoid bone

Explanation:
Thyroglossal duct cysts arise from the remnants of the thyroglossal duct (developmental tract of the thyroid gland), not the second branchial cleft. Branchial cysts arise from branchial clefts.

Question 176 (GMC - 2024)

Question:

  Canal wall down mastoidectomy
  Grommet insertion
  Myringotomy
✓ Tympanoplasty

Explanation:
For a dry central perforation with conductive loss (CSOM tubotympanic type), the surgery to repair the drum and improve hearing is Tympanoplasty (or Myringoplasty).

Question 184 (GMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
A patient with bilateral acoustic neuroma with profound bilateral sensorineural hearing loss after surgery. Which of the following is the most suitable treatment for rehabilitation of bilateral SNHL in above case

Options:
  Bilateral high power digital hearing aid
  Cochlear implants
✓ Brain stem implants
  Conventional hearing aid
  Bone anchored hearing aid

Explanation:
With bilateral acoustic neuromas (NF2) and surgery removing the cochlear nerves, cochlear implants won't work. Auditory Brainstem Implants (ABI) stimulate the cochlear nucleus directly.

Question 185 (GMC - 2024)

✓ Radical mastoidectomy
  Mastoidectomy with obliteration
  Posterior tympanotomy
  Simple mastoidectomy

Explanation:
For complications like sinus thrombosis, extensive disease clearance is priority. Radical or Modified Radical Mastoidectomy (Canal Wall Down) is typically performed to access and clear the disease.

  Arachnoid cyst

Explanation:
Progressive unilateral hearing loss and tinnitus, accompanied by cerebellar signs (falling to the left), lower cranial nerve involvement (voice change suggests vagus nerve), and facial nerve palsy (inability to close left eye, mouth deviation) are classic features of an acoustic neuroma (vestibular schwannoma), a tumor growing on the vestibulocochlear nerve (VIII) and often compressing adjacent cranial nerves.

Question 210 (KGMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 50 years old patient, known diabetic, came to the ENT-OPD with sudden onset of right ear discharge for the last 7 days. Now she cannot close her Right eye completely from 2 days and her mouth deviates to left as she smiles. What is most likely diagnosis?

Options:
  Acute otitis externa
  Bells palsy
  Middle ear malignancy
  Ramsay hunt syndrome
✓ Malignant otitis externa

Explanation:
In an elderly diabetic patient, a severe ear infection with ear discharge that progresses to facial nerve palsy (difficulty closing the eye, mouth deviation) is highly suggestive of malignant otitis externa. This aggressive infection typically caused by *Pseudomonas aeruginosa* can spread to the base of the skull, causing cranial nerve palsies.

  Right acute otitis media

The symptoms of ear blockage, severe itching, and earache, especially after swimming, along with whitish debris and mild congestion in the external auditory canal (EAC), are classic for otitis externa (swimmer's ear), an inflammation/infection of the ear canal.

Question:

  Acute tonsillitis
  Acute pharyngitis

The rapidly progressing symptoms of sore throat, high fever, drooling of saliva, stridor (difficulty breathing), and a toxic appearance with congestion of the pharynx and epiglottis are classic for acute epiglottitis, a life-threatening upper airway emergency.

A 10 years school child presented to emergency department with sore throat, odynophagia and fever with rigors and chills. On examination of the throat and tonsils were congested and covered with purulent discharge. Which pathogen most commonly involved in this condition?

  Moraxella catarrhalis

Question 214 (KGMC - 2023)

Question:

  Lateral cricoarytenoid muscle
✓ Posterior cricoarytenoid

Explanation:
The posterior cricoarytenoid muscle is the sole abductor of the vocal cords. Bilateral vocal cords in the paramedian position (partially abducted, but not fully) can cause significant airway obstruction and difficulty breathing.

Question 215 (KGMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 2 years child came to the emergency department with dyspnea, high grade fever and drooling of saliva for the last 2 days. Options:
  Staphylococcus epidermidis
  E-coli
  Moraxella catarrhalis
  Streptococcus pneumoniae
✓ Influenza type-B

Explanation:
*Haemophilus influenzae* type B (Hib) was historically the most common causative organism of acute epiglottitis, particularly in children. While Hib vaccination has dramatically reduced its incidence, it remains a possible cause, and other bacteria like *Streptococcus pneumoniae* can also cause it. The option states 'Influenza type-B', which is likely a typo and meant *Haemophilus influenzae* type B.

Question:

Options:
  Recurrent sinusitis
  Allergic rhinitis
  Adenoiditis
  Unilateral rhinolith
✓ Chronic adenotonsillitis

Explanation:
In children, chronic adenotonsillitis (enlargement of the adenoids and tonsils) is the most common anatomical cause of obstructive sleep apnea (OSA). Enlarged tonsils and adenoids physically obstruct the upper airway during sleep.

Question 217 (KGMC - 2023)

Question:
A 50 years old male, came to ENT department with hoarseness of voice and cough for the last 5 months. He is a trumpet player by profession. What is your likely diagnosis?

  Thyroglossal cyst
✓ Laryngocele
  Vocal cord polyp
  Thyroid nodule
  Laryngeal papillomatosis

Explanation:
A reducible neck swelling that increases in size with coughing or Valsalva maneuver, particularly in a trumpet player (who regularly performs Valsalva), along with hoarseness and cough, is highly characteristic of a laryngocele. This is an abnormal air-filled sac in the larynx, often communicating with the laryngeal ventricle.

Question 218 (KGMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 25-year-old male presented to the ENT OPD with a history of the intermittent swelling of the left side of the neck for one month and it was having association with intake of meals, which subsides after 2 hours. On examination there was a submandibular mass, mobile, firm and non tender. What is the most probable diagnosis?

Options:
  Sialometaplasia
✓ Sialolithiasis

Explanation:
Intermittent swelling in the submandibular region that is associated with meal intake and subsides after a few hours is a classic symptom of sialolithiasis (salivary gland stone), particularly in the submandibular gland. The stone obstructs the Wharton's duct, causing salivary stasis and swelling.

Question 219 (KGMC - 2023)

Question:
A 40 years old male known Diabetic patient presented to ENT OPD with sudden onset of swelling left face, fever and with severe pain for 2 days, on examination of the ear, nose & throat, it was diagnosed as acute suppurative parotitis, usual route of infection in this case is?

✓ Through Stensen's duct
  Through Wharton's duct
  Infection in teeth
  Infection in parotid lymph nodes

Explanation:
Acute suppurative parotitis (bacterial infection of the parotid gland), especially in a diabetic patient, typically occurs due to ascending infection from the oral cavity via the Stensen's duct (the parotid duct).

Question 220 (KGMC - 2023)

Question:
A 20-year-old boy presented to the ENT OPD with severe pain in the left ear. On clinical examination, he has vesicles on the tympanic membrane, meatal skin, concha, and post-auricular groove, and also has left-sided facial nerve palsy. What is the most probable diagnosis?

✓ Ramsay hunt syndrome

  Otitis media

The presence of vesicles on the tympanic membrane, meatal skin, concha, and post-auricular groove (herpes zoster oticus), along with ipsilateral facial nerve palsy, is the classic presentation of Ramsay Hunt syndrome (herpes zoster oticus with facial paralysis), caused by reactivation of varicella-zoster virus.

Question:

  Mucorale
  Candida
  Histoplasma
  Actinomyces

Explanation:
*Aspergillus* species (e.g., *A. niger*, *A. fumigatus*) are the most common fungal pathogens responsible for otomycosis, an opportunistic fungal infection of the external auditory canal. *Candida albicans* is another common cause.

Question:

Options:
  Congenital vocal cord paralysis
  Laryngeal web
✓ Laryngomalacia
  Laryngeal cyst
  Laryngocele

Explanation:
The stridor that increases on crying (increased airflow velocity) but typically improves in the prone position, along with direct laryngoscopic findings of an elongated, omega-shaped epiglottis that curls inward and short aryepiglottic folds, are classic diagnostic features of laryngomalacia, the most common cause of congenital stridor.

  Secretions of ceruminous glands, desquamated epithelial debris

Question 226 (KGMC - 2023)

Question:
A 28 years old school bus driver presented to ENT ward in evening with episodes of vertigo on positional change since last night. What is the commonest cause of vertigo?

  Superior semicircular canal dehiscence
✓ Benign paroxysmal positional vertigo
  Vestibular neuronitis
  Ototoxicity

Explanation:
Benign paroxysmal positional vertigo (BPPV) is the most common cause of vertigo. It is characterized by brief, episodic spells of vertigo triggered by specific head movements or changes in head position, due to dislodged otoconia in the semicircular canals.

Question 227 (KGMC - 2023)

Question:
A 17 years male present to ENT OPD with nasal blockage for the last one year. On nasal examination polyps was visible on right side. After investigations patient was labeled as a case of antrochoanal polyp. What is the treatment of choice for antrochoanal polyp?

Options:
  Antibiotics
  Steroids
✓ Functional Endoscopic sinus surgery
  Caldwell-luc operation
  Intranasal polypectomy

Explanation:
Functional Endoscopic Sinus Surgery (FESS) is the treatment of choice for antrochoanal polyps. It allows for complete removal of the polyp and its maxillary sinus attachment, minimizing recurrence, and is generally preferred over older, more invasive procedures like the Caldwell-Luc operation for this condition.

Question 228 (KGMC - 2023)

Question:
A 20 adult male patient presented to the emergency department with an acute onset of painful swallowing, fever, and difficulty opening his mouth. On examination, there was edema of the uvula and palate, a medially pushed tonsil (with normal morphology), and trismus. Most likely diagnosis:

The acute onset of painful swallowing (odynophagia), fever, trismus (difficulty opening mouth), and the specific examination findings of a medially pushed tonsil with edema of the uvula and palate are classic signs of a peritonsillar abscess, commonly known as quinsy.

Question 232 (KGMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 28-year-old female patient presented with recurrent nasal bleeding. On examination, there was septal perforation with crusting. Which one of the following is not the usual cause of septal perforation?

Options:
✓ Upper respiratory tract infection
  Syphilis
  Lupus
  Wegner's granulomatosis
  Tuberculous

Explanation:
Upper respiratory tract infections (URTIs) are common but are not typically a direct cause of septal perforation. Septal perforations are more commonly caused by trauma, chronic inflammation, autoimmune diseases (like Lupus or Wegener's granulomatosis), infections (like syphilis, tuberculosis), or iatrogenic causes (e.g., nasal surgery).

Question 233 (KGMC - 2023)

Question:
A 20-year-old male patient underwent surgery for a deviated nasal septum, and the most likely cause of postoperative saddle nose deformity with supra tip depression was:

Options:
✓ Sub mucosal resection
  Medial maxillectomy
  Septoplasty
  Functional endoscopic sinus surgery
  Cautery of the septum

Explanation:
Submucosal resection (SMR), an older technique for septal deviation, carried a higher risk of removing too much septal cartilage, compromising septal support, and leading to complications like saddle nose deformity with supratip depression.

Question 235 (KGMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 20 years old man came to ENT OPD with fever & sore throat with dribbling of saliva & trismus for only 2 days. On examination, the right tonsil is hyperemic, pushed medially & forward, and edematous uvula. There is no response to antibiotics. Rapid relief can be obtained by :

Options:
  Short course of radiotherapy
  Assurance & rest
  Antidiphtheric serum
✓ Incision & drainage
  Broad spectrum antibiotics

Explanation:
The clinical presentation (fever, sore throat, dribbling saliva, trismus, medially pushed tonsil, edematous uvula) is classic for a peritonsillar abscess (quinsy). Since it's an abscess, antibiotics alone are often insufficient, and rapid relief is achieved by incision and drainage of the pus.

Question 236 (KGMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 25-year-old female patient presented with foul smelling right ear discharge and hearing loss for 3 years. On examination, the tympanic membrane has a posterior superior marginal perforation. Which radiological investigation would you like to perform?

Options:
  Xray mastoid
  MRI
✓ CT scan of the temporal bone
  MR angiography
  CT angiogram

Explanation:
A foul-smelling ear discharge, hearing loss, and a postero-superior marginal perforation of the tympanic membrane are highly suggestive of chronic suppurative otitis media (CSOM) with cholesteatoma. A high-resolution CT scan of the temporal bone is the radiological investigation of choice for confirming and delineating the extent of cholesteatoma and bone erosion, which is crucial for surgical planning.

Question 237 (KGMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 16-year-old patient came to the emergency outpatient department with ear discharge and hearing loss for 4 months. There was perforation in the posterosuperior quadrant of the tympanic membrane with mucopurulent discharge in the ear canal. The rest of the ENT examination was unremarkable. What is your most probable diagnosis?

Options:
  Acute suppurative otitis media
✓ CSOM with squamosal disease
  Otitis media with effusion
  CSOM with mucosal disease (Note: Correct option circled seems to be d)
  Otitis externa

Explanation:
A postero-superior marginal perforation of the tympanic membrane with chronic mucopurulent discharge and hearing loss is highly suggestive of chronic suppurative otitis media (CSOM). Specifically, a posterior-superior marginal perforation is a strong indicator of cholesteatoma, which is associated with 'squamosal disease' (keratinizing squamous epithelium in the middle ear space). While 'mucosal disease' refers to non-cholesteatomatous CSOM, the marginal perforation location makes 'squamosal disease' a much stronger probability.

Question:
An 8-year-old female patient with hearing loss presented to the ENT OPD. On examination of the ear, the tympanic membrane was intact with a white shadow of mass in the anterosuperior quadrant of the tympanic membrane. What is the most probable diagnosis?

The presence of hearing loss with an intact tympanic membrane and a white shadow of a mass in the anterosuperior quadrant is highly suggestive of a congenital cholesteatoma. Congenital cholesteatomas occur behind an intact tympanic membrane, typically in the anterior superior quadrant.

Question 241 (KGMC - 2023)

Question:
A patient has a tumour of the parapharyngeal space with bulge in the oropharynx and laterally in the neck. The extent of parapharyngeal space is from the skull base up to:

Options:
  cricoid cartilage
  thyroid cartilage lower border
  hyoid
  D-Angle of mandible

Question 242 (KGMC - 2023)

Question:
A 40-year-old male presented with pain in the throat on the right side for 1 month. On examination, there was ulcerative lesions on right tonsil. A tonsillectomy has been done, and the histopathology report showed squamous cell carcinoma. What is the primary lymphatic drainage of this site?

Options:
  level III cervical lymph nodes
✓ level II cervical lymph nodes

Explanation:
The primary lymphatic drainage for the tonsils and oropharynx (including tonsillar squamous cell carcinoma) is predominantly to the ipsilateral Level II cervical lymph nodes (upper jugular nodes).

Question 243 (KGMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A post-thyroidectomy patient on her first post-operative day of surgery in the ENT ward complained of coughing while she was swallowing liquid. On examination both vocal cords were mobile. Which nerve is responsible for this condition?

Options:
  Recurrent laryngeal nerve
  External branch of Superior laryngeal nerve
✓ Internal branch of Superior laryngeal nerve
  Glossopharyngeal nerve
  Trigeminal nerve

Explanation:
Difficulty swallowing with coughing on liquids (aspiration), despite mobile vocal cords, suggests impaired sensation of the glottic and supraglottic regions. This is typically due to injury to the internal laryngeal nerve (a branch of the superior laryngeal nerve), which provides sensory innervation to these areas, leading to impaired cough reflex and aspiration.

Question 244 (KGMC - 2023)

Question:
A 12-year-old boy presented with anterior neck swelling for last one year. On examination, there was a small right thyroid lobe nodule on palpation, and FNAC shows suspicious for thyroid cancer. What thyroid malignancy is common in children?

Options:
  Follicular carcinoma
  Medullary CA
✓ Papillary CA
  anaplastic CA
  Squamous cell carcinoma

Explanation:
Papillary thyroid carcinoma is by far the most common type of thyroid malignancy in children and adolescents, typically having a good prognosis despite often presenting with lymph node metastases.

Question 245 (KGMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 40-year-old female patient presented with a large parotid swelling for 2 years. There is also a bulge in the oral cavity, pushing the tonsil medially. Ultrasound features and FNAC is suggestive of pleomorphic adenoma. What is the surgical treatment plan?

Options:
  Superficial parotidectomy
✓ Total conservative parotidectomy
  Total radical parotidectomy
  Radiotherapy
  Chemotherapy

Explanation:
Pleomorphic adenoma is the most common benign parotid tumor. The surgical treatment of choice is total conservative parotidectomy (also known as total parotidectomy with facial nerve preservation). This involves removing the entire parotid gland (or at least the superficial and deep lobes as needed for complete tumor removal) while carefully preserving the facial nerve, due to the high recurrence rate of pleomorphic adenomas with incomplete excision.

Question 246 (KGMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
Following tonsillectomy, a 17-year-old male complained of severe pain in the throat radiating towards left ear. Which nerve is responsible for post-tonsillectomy earache?

Options:
✓ IX
  X
  V
  VII
  XII

Explanation:
The glossopharyngeal nerve (Cranial Nerve IX) provides sensory innervation to both the tonsillar fossa and parts of the ear (via its tympanic branch). Therefore, irritation or inflammation in the tonsillar fossa after tonsillectomy can cause referred pain to the ear.

Question 248 (KGMC - 2023)

Question:
A 9-year-old patient presented with complaints of regurgitation of fluid through the nose and denasal speech for the last 7 days. He had previously experienced an infection. What infection is responsible for this palatal palsy in this patient?

  Acute tonsillitis
  Infectious mononucleosis
✓ Diphtheria
  Vincents angina
  acute membraneous tonsillitis

Regurgitation of fluid through the nose and denasal speech (rhinolalia aperta) are signs of palatal palsy. This neurological complication, particularly following a pharyngeal infection, is highly characteristic of diphtheria, caused by the diphtheria toxin affecting nerve function.

Question 249 (KGMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 30-year-old female presented to ENT complaining of dizziness for two days. It lasts for about 30 seconds and has no associated hearing loss. In episodic positional vertigo, which of the following test is used

Options:
  Caloric test
✓ Dix-hallpike manoeuvre
  Rotation test
  Electronystagmography
  Tympanometry

Explanation:
The description of brief, episodic dizziness (vertigo) triggered by positional changes, without associated hearing loss, is classic for benign paroxysmal positional vertigo (BPPV). The Dix-Hallpike maneuver is the diagnostic test for BPPV, designed to provoke the vertigo and observe characteristic nystagmus.

Question 250 (KGMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 42 years old female presented to ENT OPD with the history of progressive conductive hearing loss, also having pulsatile tinnitus and blood stained discharge. She also complains of headache, sweating and palpitations. What is your diagnosis?

Options:
  Acoustic neuroma
  Meniere's disease
✓ Glomus tumor
  Otosclerosis
  OME

Explanation:
Progressive conductive hearing loss, pulsatile tinnitus (a whooshing or throbbing sound synchronized with the heartbeat), and blood-stained ear discharge, along with symptoms of catecholamine excess (headache, sweating, palpitations), are classic features of a glomus tumor (paraganglioma) of the middle ear or jugular bulb. These tumors are highly vascular and can secrete catecholamines.

Question 251 (KGMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 32 years old female presented with Slowly progressive conductive hearing loss with normal tympanic membrane and Eustachian tube function. What is the most probable clinical diagnosis.

Options:
  Otitis media with effusion
  Malingering
✓ Otosclerosis
  Tympanosclerosis
  Adhesive Otitis media

Explanation:
Slowly progressive conductive hearing loss, especially in a young or middle-aged female, with an otherwise normal tympanic membrane and Eustachian tube function, is highly suggestive of otosclerosis. This condition involves abnormal bone remodeling in the otic capsule, leading to fixation of the stapes footplate.

Question 252 (KGMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 40 years old woman in third trimester referred from obstetric OPD to ENT OPD with complaints of hearing her own sounds. Examination of the ear shows movements of tympanic membrane synchronous with respiration and especially exaggerated when nostril on the contralateral side is occluded. What is your diagnosis?

Options:
  Eustachian tube obstruction
  Otitis media with effusion
  Otosclerosis
✓ Patulous Eustachian tube
  CSOM

Explanation:
Autophony (hearing one's own voice or breathing sounds abnormally loudly), tympanic membrane movements synchronous with respiration (especially exaggerated with contralateral nostril occlusion), and often occurring during pregnancy, are classic signs of a patulous Eustachian tube. This condition involves an abnormally open Eustachian tube.

================================================================================
Question 253 (KGMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 5 years old boy has been diagnosed as cholesteatoma, on examination he is having postosuperior retracton pocket in left ear. All would constitute plan of management except:

Options:
  Audiometry
  Mastoid exploration
  Tympanoplasty
✓ Myringoplasty
  Atticotomy

Explanation:
Myringoplasty is the surgical repair of a tympanic membrane perforation without addressing middle ear disease. For a cholesteatoma, which is an aggressive epithelial cyst with bone erosion, a simple myringoplasty is inadequate. Management would typically include audiometry, mastoid exploration, tympanoplasty (to reconstruct the middle ear/TM after disease removal), and atticotomy (removal of the attic wall to access cholesteatoma).

Question 254 (KGMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 30 years old male patient came with painful neck swelling and fever after the extraction of the first right lower molar five days earlier. On examination, there was swelling in the submental and submandibular regions, as well as drooling and trismus. He has difficulty in breathing for one day. What is your most probable diagnosis?

Options:
  Submandibular sialadentitis
  Submandibular sialolithiasis
  Quinsy
✓ Ludwig's angina
  Parotitis

Explanation:
The rapid onset of painful neck swelling (submental and submandibular regions), fever, drooling, trismus, and progressive difficulty breathing, all following a dental extraction, are classic signs of Ludwig's angina, a severe cellulitis of the floor of the mouth that can quickly compromise the airway.

Brief episodes of vertigo (lasting less than one minute) specifically triggered by head movements, without hearing loss or other neurological symptoms, are classic for benign paroxysmal positional vertigo (BPPV). A history of head trauma can be a predisposing factor.

Question:

✓ Lateral sinus thrombophlebitis
  Otitis hydrocephalus

The presence of cholesteatoma with high-grade fever, rigors, headache, and specific signs like tenderness along the internal jugular vein and edema over the posterior mastoid (Griesinger's sign) are classic indicators of lateral sinus thrombophlebitis, a serious intracranial complication of chronic otitis media.

Question 257 (KGMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
Regarding the normal physiology of hearing, the sound waves are perceived by brain, which of the following structure is responsible for stimulation

Options:
  Basilar membrane
  Tectorial membrane
  Meissner's membrane
✓ Sensory hair cells of organ of corti
  VIII nerve

Explanation:
In the process of hearing, sound vibrations are converted into electrical signals by the sensory hair cells located within the Organ of Corti on the basilar membrane of the cochlea. These hair cells transduce mechanical energy into neural impulses that are then transmitted via the auditory nerve (VIII nerve) to the brain.

  Tumor

Explanation:
The triad of recurrent facial paralysis (right-sided), swelling of the lips (cheilitis granulomatosa), and fissured tongue is the classic presentation of Melkersson-Rosenthal syndrome. It is a rare neurological disorder of unknown etiology.

Question 259 (KGMC - 2023)

Question:

  Acute otitis media
✓ Secretary otitis media
  Tympanosclerosis
  Otosclerosis

Explanation:
Persistence of deafness (hearing loss) after appropriate antibiotic treatment for acute otitis media, with subsidence of pain, is typical for secretory otitis media (SOM) or otitis media with effusion (OME). This condition involves the presence of fluid in the middle ear space without signs of acute infection, causing conductive hearing loss.

Question:

Options:
  Suction clearance of the tube
  Humidified O2 inhalation
  Racemic Epinephrine Nebulization
  Use of Soda Bicarbonate and suction clearance
✓ Immediate removal of the tracheostomy tube

Explanation:
In a tracheostomized patient with sudden, complete tracheostomy tube blockage and difficulty breathing, immediate removal of the tube is the most critical first step. This re-establishes an airway, allowing for reinsertion of a new tube or other airway management. Attempts at suctioning might dislodge the obstruction further down or be insufficient.

Question:
A 3 year-old child presented to ENT Emergency department with history of coughing, choking and gagging for the last 2 days. On Xray chest, there was right hyper inflated chest shadow with trachea deviated to the opposite side. What is your most likely diagnosis?

  Acute Bronchial Asthma
  Bronchiectasis
✓ Foreign Body in the Right Bronchus
  Acute respiratory Distress syndrome

Explanation:
The acute onset of coughing, choking, and gagging, followed by unilateral reduced breath sounds, unilateral wheeze, hyperinflated chest, and tracheal deviation on X-ray in a child, are classic signs of a foreign body lodged in the right bronchus, causing a check-valve obstruction (air trapping).

Question 265 (KGMC - 2023)

Question:

Options:
  Antibiotics
  Wait and see
✓ Speech therapy
  Microlaryngoscopic excision & Speech therapy
  Microlaryngoscopic excision

Explanation:
Vocal cord nodules ('singer's nodules') are benign lesions caused by vocal abuse. For persistent nodules in a professional voice user, speech therapy (voice therapy) is the first-line and most effective treatment. Surgical excision is reserved for cases that fail conservative management.

Question 268 (KGMC - 2023)

Question:
A 50-year-old patient had hoarseness of voice and coughing on fluid intake for three months. On endoscopic examination, the right vocal cord was in the cadaveric position. Which nerves are involved in this condition?

Options:
  Right superior Laryngeal nerve
  Right internal laryngeal branch of superior laryngeal nerve
  Right recurrent laryngeal nerve of vagus nerve
✓ Recurrent and superior laryngeal branches of vagus nerve
  Non recurrent laryngeal nerve

Explanation:
A vocal cord in the 'cadaveric' position (intermediate position) with hoarseness and coughing on fluid intake (aspiration) suggests complete paralysis of the vocal cord. This involves damage to both the recurrent laryngeal nerve (affecting all intrinsic laryngeal muscles except cricothyroid) and the superior laryngeal nerve (affecting cricothyroid and laryngeal sensation), as both contribute to vocal cord function and protection.

Question 272 (KGMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 30 years old female patient is complaining of bilateral progressive hearing loss with no history of ear discharge or trauma. On examination she is having bilateral intact TMs. On PTA she is having conductive hearing loss. What type of tympanogram she will have?

Options:
  Ad
✓ As
  A
  B
  C

Explanation:
Bilateral progressive conductive hearing loss with intact tympanic membranes and a normal Eustachian tube function is highly suggestive of otosclerosis. In otosclerosis, the stapes footplate becomes fixed, leading to reduced compliance of the middle ear system, which results in a Type As tympanogram (shallow, stiff curve).

  Ac polyps
✓ Septal hematoma
  Choanal atresia
  Septal perforation

Explanation:
Bilateral, smooth, rounded, soft, and fluctuant swelling of the nasal septum following facial trauma is the classic presentation of a septal hematoma. This is a collection of blood under the perichondrium of the nasal septum and requires urgent drainage to prevent complications.

Question 274 (KGMC - 2023)

Question:
A 22 years old female patient presented with bleeding from right side of the nose for last 2 days. On examination she was having raw area on the right septum. What is the most common site of epistaxis?

Options:

  Inferior turbinate
✓ Little's area
  Posterior septum
  Diffuse

Explanation:
Little's area (or Kiesselbach's plexus) on the anterior nasal septum is the most common site of epistaxis (nosebleeds), especially in younger individuals. It is a vascular watershed area prone to trauma and dryness, leading to bleeding.

Question 279 (KGMC - 2023)

Question:
...soft palate shows inflammation with bullous eruptions. Taste sensations over the Left side of tongue are absent. Tuning fork test shows the Weber lateralizing to the Left ear. What is the most likely diagnosis in this patient?

Options:
  Bullous Myringitis
  Diffuse otitis externa
  Herpangina
✓ Ramsay- Hunt Syndrome
  Steven-Johnson Syndrome

Explanation:
The presence of vesicles/bullous eruptions on the soft palate, altered taste sensation on the left side of the tongue, and Weber lateralizing to the left ear (suggesting left-sided sensorineural hearing loss or significant conductive loss) along with ear symptoms from the previous question, are all features that can coalesce into a diagnosis of Ramsay Hunt syndrome (herpes zoster oticus), which affects cranial nerves VII and VIII, and sometimes IX and X.

Question 280 (KGMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 6 months old male child was brought to ENT OPD with history of stridor. Stridor increases with crying but subsides on placing the child in prone position and his cry is normal. Direct laryngoscopy shows an omega shaped epiglottis. What is the most probable diagnosis?

Options:
  Subglottic stenosis
  Laryngeal web
  Vocal cord palsy
✓ Laryngomalacia
  acute epiglottitis

Explanation:
The stridor that increases with crying (increased airflow velocity) but improves in the prone position (gravity pulls supraglottic structures forward), a normal cry, and the laryngoscopic finding of an omega-shaped epiglottis are classic diagnostic features of laryngomalacia, the most common cause of congenital stridor.

Question 281 (KGMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 3 year old child has put a button battery in the nose 6 hours back. He is now having nasal obstruction and nasal discharge. On examination; anterior rhinoscopy revealed no foreign body. The left nostril shows reduced patency on cold spatula test.. X-ray nose AP and lateral views confirms the presence of foreign body. While you are working in a tertiary care hospital, what is the preferred choice for removal of this foreign body?

Options:
  Wrap the child in a towel and remove with hook.
  Wrap the child in a towel and remove with nook and endoscope
  Remove with hook under rigid endoscopic control under LA
  Intubate the child and remove using blunt hook under GA
✓ Intubate the child and remove using endoscope under GA

Explanation:
A button battery in the nose is a medical emergency due to the rapid liquefaction necrosis it can cause. Given the child's age, the need for complete and atraumatic removal of a potentially corrosive foreign body, and the presence in a tertiary care hospital, intubation of the child and removal using an endoscope under general anesthesia (GA) is the preferred and safest choice. This allows for controlled removal, prevents aspiration, and permits thorough inspection for damage.

Question 282 (KGMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 22 year old girl presented with sore throat, foreign body sensation and occasional episodes of hoarseness for the past 3 years. The symptoms get worse whenever she has to appear in exams. On examination; The tonsillar area and posterior pharyngeal wall are congested. Endoscopic direct laryngoscopy shows no pooling of saliva, congested but mobile vocal cords with signs of acute inflammation in the interarytenoid area.   Globus Pharyngis
  Plummer- Vinson's syndrome
✓ Gastro-oesophageal reflux disease
  Intubation granuloma

Explanation:
The chronic symptoms of sore throat, foreign body sensation ('globus sensation'), hoarseness, and exacerbation during stress (exams) are highly suggestive of laryngopharyngeal reflux (LPR), which is often a manifestation of gastro-oesophageal reflux disease (GORD). Question 283 (KGMC - 2023)

Question:
A 33 year old lady presented to ENT OPD with fever, severe pain in her Left ear, difficulty in swallowing and altered taste for the last 4 days. On examination; her temp is 100F. The Left pinna, external auditory and tympanic membrane appear inflamed. The Left side of soft palate shows inflammation...

  Steven-Johnson Syndrome

Explanation:

Question 288 (KGMC - 2023)

Question:
During Thyroidectomy, care should be taken to preserve the superior laryngeal nerve along with the recurrent laryngeal nerve. Which of the following muscle is innervated by superior laryngeal nerve?

Options:
✓ Cricothyroid
  Lateral Cricoarytenoid
  Posterior cricoarytenoid
  Transverse arytenoids
  Inter arytenoids

Question 289 (KGMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 60-year-old male patient presented with palpable nodes in the right posterior triangle of the neck. In addition, he has right-sided nasal obstruction and ear fullness. FNAC of the node is suggestive of metastatic disease. Likelihood... nodal metastasis to the posterior triangle of the neck is from:

Options:
  Buccal mucosa
  Hard palate
  Paranasal sinuses
✓ Nasopharynx
  Glottis

Explanation:
Metastatic lymph nodes in the posterior triangle of the neck, especially when associated with symptoms like nasal obstruction and ear fullness (suggesting Eustachian tube dysfunction), are highly suspicious for a primary tumor in the nasopharynx (e.g., nasopharyngeal carcinoma). Nasopharyngeal carcinoma frequently metastasizes to the upper cervical lymph nodes, including the posterior triangle.

  Anterior Ethmoid

  Posterior Ethmoid

Question 291 (KGMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A young male patient came to ENT-OPD with ear pain, fever, and hearing loss for one week. On examination, the tympanic membrane was congested with pulsatile otorrhea. This is usually seen in:

Options:
  Glomus tumour
  CSF otorrhea
✓ Acute Suppurative otitis media
  Fistula
  CSOM

Explanation:
Pulsatile otorrhea (ear discharge that pulsates) with a congested tympanic membrane, ear pain, fever, and hearing loss, suggests a vigorous inflammatory process in the middle ear, such as acute suppurative otitis media (AOM) or an exacerbation of chronic suppurative otitis media (CSOM). While glomus tumors can cause pulsatile tinnitus, the context of an acute inflammatory presentation points more towards AOM or CSOM.

Question 292 (KGMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 60-year-old male patient is diagnosed with squamous cell carcinoma of the larynx with involvement of the thyroid cartilage, and it is staged as T4N0M0. What will be the best treatment?

Options:
  Radiotherapy
  Chemotherapy
  Total laryngectomy
✓ Total laryngectomy with radiotherapy
  Neck dissection

Explanation:
For advanced laryngeal squamous cell carcinoma with involvement of the thyroid cartilage (T4 stage), the best treatment typically involves a combination of total laryngectomy (surgical removal of the larynx) and adjuvant radiotherapy, often with or without chemotherapy. This aggressive approach aims for curative intent and addresses local and regional disease spread.

  Postcricoid growth

Question 294 (KGMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 25-year-old woman was referred from the Obstetric Department to the Ear, Nose and Throat Department with a history of bleeding from the oral cavity; on examination, she has a soft, smooth, reddish to purple mass on the anterior dorsum of the tongue that bleeds on touch. What is the most probable diagnosis?

Options:
  Hemangioma
  Papilloma
✓ Pyogenic granuloma
  Fibroepithelial polyp
  Granular cell myoblastoma

Explanation:
A soft, smooth, reddish to purple mass on the tongue that bleeds easily on touch, particularly in a pregnant woman (or due to hormonal changes), is highly suggestive of a pyogenic granuloma (also known as a lobular capillary hemangioma). These lesions are benign and vascular, often growing rapidly.

================================================================================
Question 295 (KGMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 17-year-old boy visited the Ear, Nose, and Throat Department with a history of persistent nasal obstruction and recurrent epistaxis for the last 5 months. To exclude angiofibroma, What will be the investigation of choice?

Options:
  Carotid angiography
✓ CT Scan of nose and paranasal sinuses with contrast
  MRI of nose and paranasal sinuses
  Xray Paranasal sinuses
  Xray neck lateral view

Explanation:
In an adolescent male with persistent nasal obstruction and recurrent epistaxis, juvenile nasopharyngeal angiofibroma (JNA) should be suspected. A CT scan of the nose and paranasal sinuses with contrast is the investigation of choice for initial diagnosis, to define the tumor's extent, and assess its vascularity. Angiography may be used for embolization, but CT is for initial diagnosis.

================================================================================
Question 296 (KGMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 35-year-old man gives a history of unilateral clear discharge from the nose for the last 6 months, which increases on bending the head forward. He had history of road traffic accident and remained unconscious for some time. To rule out CSF rhinorrhea, Which of the following laboratory test is specifically recommended?

Options:
  CSF sugar
  Serum electrolytes
  Serum albumin
✓ B2 transferin
  Blood sugar

Explanation:
Unilateral clear nasal discharge, especially increasing on bending forward and following head trauma, is highly suspicious for cerebrospinal fluid (CSF) rhinorrhea. The most specific laboratory test to confirm CSF rhinorrhea is the detection of beta-2 transferrin (or beta-trace protein) in the nasal discharge, as these are uniquely found in CSF.

================================================================================
Question 297 (KGMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
An adult patient presented with a painful swelling at the tip of his nose and fever for two days. On examination, the nasal vestibule was erythematous and tender to touch. The condition was diagnosed as nasal furunculosis? Which one of the following pathogen is involved in this condition?

Options:
  streptococcus viridans
✓ Staphylococcus aureus
  Pneumococcus
  E coli.
  B proteus.

Explanation:
Nasal furunculosis (a painful boil or abscess in the nasal vestibule) is almost always caused by *Staphylococcus aureus*, which is a common commensal on the skin and in the anterior nares. The painful swelling, erythema, and tenderness are characteristic.

================================================================================
Question 298 (KGMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 30 years old male patient had recurrent attacks of pain in the right ear with blockage for 8 weeks. It was diagnosed as right chronic otitis externa. Which of the following organisms is responsible for this disease?

Options:
✓ Pseudomonas Aeruginosa
  Staph Aureus
  Morexella Cattarhalis
  Bacteriods
  H. Influenza

Explanation:
*Pseudomonas aeruginosa* is a common causative organism for chronic otitis externa, especially in cases that are recurrent or associated with moisture/swimming. *Staphylococcus aureus* is also frequent, but *Pseudomonas* is a very typical cause of chronic/recalcitrant OE.

================================================================================
Question 299 (KGMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 25-year-old female patient presented with bilateral hearing loss for 1 month. On otoscopic examination, there was a reddish blush or sun rising appearance on the promontory, and on a pure tone audiogram, there was bilateral mild conductive hearing loss with a dip at the 2000 Hz frequency. What is your most likely diagnosis?

Options:
  Otitis media with effusion
  Otitis media
  Cholesterol granuloma
✓ Otosclerosis
  Meniere's disease

Explanation:
Bilateral progressive conductive hearing loss, an intact tympanic membrane, and the classic 'reddish blush' or 'sunrise appearance' (Schwartze's sign) on the promontory are pathognomonic for otosclerosis. The dip at 2000 Hz (Carhart's notch) is also characteristic of otosclerosis, reflecting a bone conduction artifact.

================================================================================
Question 300 (KGMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A male adult patient came with complaints of swelling in front of the left ear for 2 months. On examination, it was non tender, mobile, firm in consistency, and without facial palsy. The rest of the neck examination was unremarkable. What is your most probable diagnosis?

Options:
  Hemangioma of the parotid
  Lipoma
  Warthin tumor
  Parotid cyst
✓ pleomorphic adenoma

Explanation:
A slowly growing, non-tender, mobile, firm swelling in front of the ear (parotid region), without facial nerve involvement, is the classic presentation of a pleomorphic adenoma, which is the most common benign tumor of the parotid gland.

================================================================================
Question 301 (KGMC - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
A 60-year-old male presented to ENT ER with upper neck swelling bilaterally and difficulty in breathing for 1 week. In examination there was a bulge in floor of mouth and it was diagnosed with Ludwig’s angina. Infection of which space?

Options:
  buccal space
  parotid space
✓ submandibular space
  diagastric space
  buccinator space

Explanation:
Ludwig's angina is a potentially life-threatening cellulitis of the floor of the mouth, involving the submandibular, submental, and sublingual spaces. It causes bilateral neck swelling and elevation of the tongue (bulge in floor of mouth).

Question 302 (KGMC - 2025)

Question:
5-year-old child presented to OPD with hoarseness of voice, difficulty in breathing and inspiratory stridor. Diagnosed with juvenile laryngeal papillomatosis. Most common virus?

Options:
  EBV
✓ HPV-11
  CMV
  VZV
  Herpes simplex type 1

Explanation:
Recurrent Respiratory Papillomatosis (Juvenile Laryngeal Papillomatosis) is caused by Human Papillomavirus (HPV), most commonly types 6 and 11. It presents with hoarseness and stridor in children.

Question 303 (KGMC - 2025)

Question:
30-year-old man with tinnitus, vertigo, intermittent attacks of hearing loss, nausea, and vomiting. Diagnosis?

Options:
  Vestibular Neuronitis
  Benign Paroxysmal Positional Vertigo (BPPV)
✓ Meniere’s disease
  Acoustic Neuroma
  Labyrinthitis

Explanation:
The classic triad of Meniere's disease consists of episodic vertigo, fluctuating sensorineural hearing loss, and tinnitus (often with aural fullness).

Question 304 (KGMC - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
Pleomorphic adenoma: right treatment of choice?

Options:
  Enucleation
✓ Superficial parotidectomy
  Total parotidectomy
  Radiotherapy
  Chemotherapy

Explanation:
The standard treatment for pleomorphic adenoma is superficial parotidectomy. Simple enucleation is contraindicated due to the high risk of recurrence caused by microscopic extensions of the tumor through its pseudocapsule.

Question 305 (KGMC - 2025)

Question:

✓ Foreign body inhalation
  Lower respiratory tract infection
  Pneumothorax

Explanation:
In a young child, recurrent unilateral pneumonia or unresolved lobar opacity with decreased air entry is highly suggestive of an aspirated foreign body causing bronchial obstruction.

Question 306 (KGMC - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
30-year-old male with nasal obstruction for 2 years, bilateral hypertrophied turbinates. Middle turbinate is part of which bone?

Options:
  Nasal
  Maxilla
✓ Ethmoid
  Sphenoid
  Palatine

Explanation:
The superior and middle nasal turbinates are bony projections of the ethmoid bone. The inferior nasal turbinate is a separate bone.

Question 307 (KGMC - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
Baby born with respiratory insufficiency and cyanotic spells on feeding, treated with McGovern’s nipple. Congenital anomaly?

Options:
  unilateral choanal atresia
✓ bilateral choanal atresia
  Mobius syndrome
  Usher syndrome
  Pendred syndrome

Explanation:
Bilateral choanal atresia presents at birth with cyclical cyanosis (blue when feeding/quiet, pink when crying). Because neonates are obligate nose breathers, this is an emergency. McGovern’s nipple helps maintain an oral airway.

Question 308 (KGMC - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
30-year-old lady with pleomorphic adenoma removed via superficial parotidectomy. Weeks later sweating around right face while eating. Diagnosis?

Options:
  food allergy
✓ Frey's syndrome
  general hyperhidrosis
  compensatory hyperhidrosis
  gustatory sweating

Explanation:
Frey's syndrome (gustatory sweating) is a complication of parotidectomy where regenerating parasympathetic fibers (secretomotor to saliva glands) aberrantly innervate the sweat glands of the overlying skin.

Question 309 (KGMC - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
60-year-old patient with SCC larynx, thyroid cartilage involved, T4N0M0. Best treatment?

Options:
  Radiotherapy only
  Chemotherapy only
✓ Total laryngectomy
  Partial laryngectomy
  Laser excision

Explanation:
T4 laryngeal carcinoma typically involves invasion of the thyroid cartilage or extra-laryngeal spread. The standard treatment to ensure clear margins and cure is a total laryngectomy, often followed by chemoradiotherapy.

Question 310 (KGMC - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
35-year-old female teacher with hoarseness for 3 months, lesions on anterior 1/3 of vocal cords. Diagnosis?

Options:
  Chronic laryngitis
  Reinke's edema
✓ Vocal cord nodules
  Vocal cord hemangioma

Explanation:
Vocal cord nodules (screamer's nodules) typically occur at the junction of the anterior 1/3 and posterior 2/3 of the vocal cords and are common in professionals who use their voice extensively (like teachers).

Question 311 (KGMC - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
Diabetic patient, acute suppurative parotitis. Usual route of infection?

Options:
✓ Stensen duct
  Wharton's duct
  Hematogenous spread
  Lymphatic spread
  Direct extension

Explanation:
Acute suppurative parotitis is usually caused by the retrograde migration of oral bacteria (commonly Staph. aureus) via the parotid (Stensen's) duct, often due to dehydration or poor oral hygiene.

Question 312 (KGMC - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
60-year-old diabetic female with severe ear pain suggesting malignant otitis externa. Complications except?

Options:
  Intracranial complication
  Facial nerve palsy
  Skull base fracture
✓ Distant metastasis
  External auditory canal stenosis

Explanation:
Malignant Otitis Externa is an aggressive osteomyelitis (infection) of the skull base, not a neoplasm (cancer). Therefore, it causes local destruction and nerve palsies but does not result in distant metastasis.

Question 313 (KGMC - 2025)

Options:
  Intranasal steroid spray
✓ Xylometazoline
  Ipratropium bromide
  Antihistamine
  Cocaine

Explanation:
Rhinitis medicamentosa is rebound congestion caused by the prolonged use (usually >5-7 days) of topical nasal decongestants like Xylometazoline or Oxymetazoline.

✓ Hypertrophy of adenoids and tonsils

Adenotonsillar hypertrophy is the most common cause of Obstructive Sleep Apnea (OSA) in children, causing physical obstruction of the airway during sleep.

Question 318 (KGMC - 2025)

Hypopharynx subsites include all except?

Explanation:
The hypopharynx consists of the piriform fossa (sinus), the postcricoid area, and the posterior pharyngeal wall. The epiglottis is part of the larynx (supraglottis).

Question 320 (KGMC - 2025)

Question:

Question 322 (KGMC - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
Conductive hearing loss: Weber lateralizes to?

Options:
✓ Affected ear
  Normal ear
  Both ears
  Neither ear
  Alternating ears

Explanation:
In Conductive Hearing Loss (CHL), the Weber test lateralizes to the affected (worse) ear because external masking noise is blocked, making the bone-conducted sound seem louder in that ear.

Question 323 (KGMC - 2025)

Question:
22-year-old male with nasal trauma 10 days back, no mucosal hematoma, external deformity. Treatment?

Options:
  FESS
  SMR
  Septoplasty
  open septorhinoplasty
✓ Reduction & splintage

Explanation:
For a fractured nasal bone with deformity, closed reduction is the standard treatment. It is best performed before the bones unite, typically within 2 weeks of the injury.

Most common pathogen for epiglottitis?

Question 325 (KGMC - 2025)

Question:

  Paranasal sinuses
✓ Nasopharynx
  Glottis

Nasopharyngeal carcinoma is well-known for metastasizing to the posterior triangle lymph nodes. It also blocks the Eustachian tube (ear fullness) and nasal choanae (obstruction).

Question 328 (KGMC - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
Which muscle becomes paralyzed in superior laryngeal nerve palsy?

Options:
✓ Cricothyroid
  Thyroarytenoid
  Lateral Cricoarytenoid
  Transverse arytenoid
  Posterior cricoarytenoid

Explanation:
The external branch of the Superior Laryngeal Nerve supplies the cricothyroid muscle (the 'tuner' of the larynx). Its paralysis leads to an inability to produce high-pitched sounds.

Question 329 (KGMC - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
A 25-year-old female presents with progressive hearing loss in both ears, worse on the right. She reports her hearing improves in noisy environments (Paracusis Willisii). Otoscopy shows a normal tympanic membrane with a faint reddish hue (Schwartz sign). Diagnosis?

Options:
✓ Otosclerosis
  Tympanosclerosis
  Presbycusis
  Meniere's disease
  Chronic Suppurative Otitis Media

Explanation:
Otosclerosis is a bony labyrinth disease causing fixation of the stapes footplate. It typically affects young females and causes conductive hearing loss. Paracusis Willisii and Schwartz sign (flamingo pink blush) are pathognomonic.

================================================================================
Question 330 (KGMC - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
A 14-year-old boy presents with severe, recurrent epistaxis and nasal obstruction. Anterior rhinoscopy reveals a reddish mass in the nasopharynx. Biopsy is contraindicated. What is the most likely diagnosis?

Options:
  Antrochoanal polyp
✓ Juvenile Nasopharyngeal Angiofibroma
  Nasopharyngeal carcinoma
  Hemangioma
  Inverted papilloma

Explanation:
Juvenile Nasopharyngeal Angiofibroma (JNA) occurs exclusively in adolescent males. It is highly vascular, causing profuse bleeding. Biopsy is contraindicated before imaging/embolization due to the risk of massive hemorrhage.

================================================================================
Question 331 (KGMC - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
A 45-year-old female complains of difficulty swallowing, fatigue, and spoon-shaped nails (koilonychia). Investigations reveal iron deficiency anemia. She is at increased risk for which malignancy?

Options:
✓ Post-cricoid carcinoma
  Nasopharyngeal carcinoma
  Thyroid carcinoma
  Carcinoma of the tongue
  Laryngeal carcinoma

Explanation:
This is the triad of Plummer-Vinson (Paterson-Brown-Kelly) syndrome: Dysphagia (due to esophageal webs), Iron deficiency anemia, and Glossitis. It is a premalignant condition for post-cricoid carcinoma.

================================================================================
Question 332 (KGMC - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
A patient presents with severe throat pain, 'hot potato' voice, trismus, and drooling. Examination shows the uvula deviated to the healthy side and a bulging soft palate. Diagnosis?

Options:
  Acute tonsillitis
✓ Peritonsillar abscess (Quinsy)
  Retropharyngeal abscess
  Parapharyngeal abscess
  Diphtheria

Explanation:
Peritonsillar abscess (Quinsy) is a collection of pus between the tonsil capsule and the superior constrictor muscle. Classic signs include trismus, hot potato voice, and uvular deviation away from the lesion.

Question 333 (KGMC - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
During a thyroidectomy, a patient develops unilateral vocal cord paralysis. The cord is fixed in the paramedian position. Which nerve is injured?

Options:
  External laryngeal nerve
✓ Recurrent laryngeal nerve
  Internal laryngeal nerve
  Glossopharyngeal nerve
  Hypoglossal nerve

Explanation:
The Recurrent Laryngeal Nerve (RLN) supplies all intrinsic muscles of the larynx except the cricothyroid. Unilateral injury results in the vocal cord being fixed in the paramedian position.

Question 334 (KGMC - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
A patient involved in a road traffic accident has clear fluid leaking from the nose that increases when bending forward. The fluid tests positive for Beta-2 transferrin. Diagnosis?

Options:
  Allergic rhinitis
  Vasomotor rhinitis
✓ CSF Rhinorrhea
  Nasal polyp secretion
  Lacrimal fluid leak

Explanation:
Clear rhinorrhea following head trauma that is positive for Beta-2 transferrin (a protein found almost exclusively in CSF) confirms the diagnosis of Cerebrospinal Fluid (CSF) Rhinorrhea.

Question 335 (KGMC - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
A 6-year-old child presents with hearing loss and poor school performance. Otoscopy reveals dull, retracted tympanic membranes with air bubbles. Tympanometry shows a flat curve (Type B). Diagnosis?

Options:
  Acute Otitis Media
✓ Otitis Media with Effusion (Glue Ear)
  Chronic Suppurative Otitis Media
  Otosclerosis
  Foreign body in ear

Explanation:
Otitis Media with Effusion (OME), or Glue Ear, is common in children due to Eustachian tube dysfunction. It causes conductive hearing loss without signs of acute infection (fever/pain). Type B tympanogram indicates fluid.

Question 336 (KGMC - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
A 50-year-old male complains of short episodes of vertigo lasting seconds, triggered by turning over in bed. There is no hearing loss. The Dix-Hallpike maneuver induces nystagmus. Diagnosis?

Options:
  Meniere's disease
  Vestibular Neuronitis
  Labyrinthitis
✓ Benign Paroxysmal Positional Vertigo (BPPV)
  Acoustic Neuroma

Explanation:
BPPV is characterized by brief vertigo triggered by head position changes, caused by otoliths (canalithiasis) in the semicircular canals. It is diagnosed via the Dix-Hallpike test and treated with the Epley maneuver.

Question 337 (KGMC - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
A patient presents with sudden onset of pain and swelling in the submandibular region while eating. The swelling subsides after meals. Most likely cause?

Options:
  Mumps
✓ Sialolithiasis
  Sjogren's syndrome
  Pleomorphic adenoma
  Ludwig's angina

Explanation:
Sialolithiasis (salivary stones) causes obstruction of the duct. Symptoms typically include 'mealtime syndrome'—pain and swelling during eating when saliva production is stimulated against the obstruction.

Question 338 (KGMC - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
In a patient with epistaxis, the most common site of bleeding is the anterior part of the nasal septum. This area is known as:

Options:
  Woodruff's plexus
✓ Little's area (Kiesselbach's plexus)
  Retro-columellar vein
  Turbinate plexus
  Sphenopalatine artery zone

Explanation:
Little's area (Kiesselbach's plexus) is located on the anterior cartilaginous septum. It is the anastomosis of four arteries and is the site for 90% of epistaxis cases, especially in children.
