Question 339 (KGMC - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
A newborn presents with inspiratory stridor that worsens when crying or lying supine and improves when prone. Direct laryngoscopy shows an omega-shaped epiglottis. Diagnosis?

Options:
  Subglottic stenosis
  Laryngeal web
✓ Laryngomalacia
  Vocal cord palsy
  Laryngeal cyst

Explanation:
Laryngomalacia is the most common cause of congenital stridor. It results from flaccidity of the supraglottic structures (omega epiglottis, short aryepiglottic folds) which collapse during inspiration.

================================================================================
Question 340 (KGMC - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
An elderly patient presents with regurgitation of undigested food eaten days ago, halitosis, and a lump in the neck that gurgles on compression. Barium swallow shows a pouch. Diagnosis?

Options:
  Achalasia
  Hiatal hernia
✓ Pharyngeal Pouch (Zenker's Diverticulum)
  Esophageal web
  Esophageal carcinoma

Explanation:
Zenker's diverticulum is a herniation of the pharyngeal mucosa through Killian's dehiscence (between thyropharyngeus and cricopharyngeus). It causes dysphagia, regurgitation, and a gurgling neck mass.

================================================================================
Question 341 (KGMC - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
A 20-year-old male presents with a midline neck swelling that moves up on protrusion of the tongue. Diagnosis?

Options:
  Dermoid cyst
✓ Thyroglossal cyst
  Branchial cyst
  Lipoma
  Thyroid nodule

Explanation:
A Thyroglossal Duct Cyst is a congenital remnant of the thyroid's descent. Its attachment to the foramen caecum at the base of the tongue causes it to move upward when the tongue is protruded.

Question 342 (KGMC - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
A diabetic patient presents with blackish, necrotic crusts in the nasal cavity, proptosis, and ophthalmoplegia. KOH mount shows broad aseptate hyphae. Diagnosis?

Options:
  Invasive Aspergillosis
✓ Mucormycosis
  Rhinoscleroma
  Wegener's granulomatosis
  Nasal NK/T-cell lymphoma

Explanation:
Invasive fungal rhinosinusitis (Mucormycosis) is an emergency typical in immunocompromised or uncontrolled diabetic patients. It causes tissue necrosis (black eschar) and invades the orbit/brain.

Question 343 (KGMC - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
A patient with Chronic Suppurative Otitis Media (CSOM) presents with vertigo and a positive Fistula test. Which structure is most likely eroded?

Options:
✓ Lateral semicircular canal
  Superior semicircular canal
  Posterior semicircular canal
  Cochlea
  Fallopian canal

Explanation:
Cholesteatoma commonly erodes the bony labyrinth, most frequently the Lateral Semicircular Canal. This creates a fistula, transmitting pressure changes to the inner ear (positive Fistula sign).

================================================================================
Question 344 (KGMC - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
Which of the following drugs is notoriously ototoxic, causing irreversible sensorineural hearing loss?

Options:
  Amoxicillin
✓ Gentamicin
  Paracetamol
  Prednisolone
  Acyclovir

Explanation:
Aminoglycosides (like Gentamicin, Streptomycin) are ototoxic, causing damage to hair cells in the cochlea (hearing loss) and vestibule (balance issues).

================================================================================
Question 345 (KGMC - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
A patient presents with a painful, shallow ulcer with a yellow base and red halo on the labial mucosa. It recurs every few months and heals without scarring. Diagnosis?

Options:
  Squamous cell carcinoma
✓ Minor Aphthous Ulcer
  Herpetic gingivostomatitis
  Syphilitic ulcer
  Traumatic ulcer

Explanation:
Minor Aphthous Ulcers (canker sores) are common, painful, recurrent oral ulcers usually <1cm, found on non-keratinized mucosa, and heal spontaneously within 7-10 days.

Question 346 (KGMC - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
A patient with acute sinusitis develops swelling of the eyelids and restricted eye movements. What is the likely complication?

Options:
  Pott's puffy tumor
✓ Orbital cellulitis
  Cavernous sinus thrombosis
  Mucocele
  Dacrocystitis

Explanation:
Orbital cellulitis is a serious complication of ethmoid sinusitis. Infection breaches the lamina papyracea, causing proptosis, chemosis, and ophthalmoplegia.

Question 347 (KGMC - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
Facial trauma resulting in a 'floating palate' and separation of the alveolus/palate from the rest of the face is classified as:

Options:
✓ Le Fort I fracture
  Le Fort II fracture
  Le Fort III fracture
  Zygomatic fracture
  Nasal bone fracture

Explanation:
Le Fort I (Guerin fracture) is a horizontal fracture above the roots of the teeth, separating the palate/alveolus from the maxilla, creating a 'floating palate'.

Question 348 (KGMC - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
In a patient with unilateral sensorineural hearing loss and poor speech discrimination, MRI is ordered to rule out:

Options:
  Otosclerosis
✓ Vestibular Schwannoma (Acoustic Neuroma)
  Meniere's disease
  Presbycusis
  Glomus tumor

Explanation:
Asymmetric sensorineural hearing loss is the hallmark of Vestibular Schwannoma (Acoustic Neuroma). It is a retrocochlear lesion, so speech discrimination is disproportionately poor compared to pure tone thresholds.

Question 349 (KGMC - 2025)

Question:
A 25-year-old presents with a painless cystic swelling along the anterior border of the sternocleidomastoid muscle. Aspiration yields cholesterol crystals. Diagnosis?

Options:
  Cystic Hygroma
✓ Branchial Cyst
  Thyroglossal Cyst
  Ranula
  Laryngocele

Explanation:
Branchial cysts usually arise from the second branchial cleft. They present in young adults along the anterior border of the SCM. The presence of cholesterol crystals in the aspirate is diagnostic.

Question 350 (KGMC - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
Emergency tracheostomy is typically performed at which level of the trachea?

Options:
  Cricothyroid membrane
  Between 1st and 2nd tracheal rings
✓ Between 2nd and 3rd tracheal rings
  Between 5th and 6th tracheal rings
  Below the thyroid isthmus

Explanation:
Standard tracheostomy is performed between the 2nd and 3rd (or 3rd and 4th) tracheal rings to avoid subglottic stenosis (if too high) or vessel injury (if too low).

Question 351 (KGMC - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
A heavy smoker female presents with a deep, low-pitched masculine voice. Laryngoscopy shows bilateral diffuse edema of the vocal cords. Diagnosis?

Options:
  Vocal nodules
✓ Reinke's Edema
  Laryngeal papilloma
  Laryngeal web
  Contact ulcer

Explanation:
Reinke's edema (Polypoid degeneration) involves fluid accumulation in the subepithelial space (Reinke's space) of the vocal cords. It is strongly associated with smoking and causes a low-pitched voice.

Question:

✓ Atrophic Rhinitis (Ozaena)
  Rhinitis sicca
  Syphilitic rhinitis

Explanation:
Atrophic rhinitis (Ozaena) is characterized by progressive atrophy of the nasal mucosa and bone. Despite a roomy cavity, patients complain of obstruction (loss of sensation) and foul smell (cacosmia/ozena).

✓ Diphtheria

Question 354 (KGMC - 2025)

Question:
Which audiological test is best used to confirm the presence of recruitment in Meniere’s disease?

Options:
  Tone decay test
✓ Short Increment Sensitivity Index (SISI)
  Rinne test
  Bing test
  Stenger test

Explanation:
Recruitment (abnormal growth of loudness) is a feature of cochlear pathology like Meniere's. The SISI test measures the ability to detect small changes in intensity; a high score indicates recruitment.

Options:
  T1a
  T1b
  T2
✓ T3
  T4

Explanation:
In glottic carcinoma, T1 is limited to the cords with normal mobility, T2 involves supraglottis/subglottis with impaired mobility, and T3 causes vocal cord fixation.

Question 360 (KMC - 2023)

Question:

Question 361 (KMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 15 years old patient has presented to a consultant clinic for complaining of right ear discharge, swelling and pain for last 20 days. The consultant notices a swelling and labeled it as an extracranial complication of choronic supportive otitis media along the sterocleidomastoid muscle. CT Scans shows erosion of the bone at sinodural angle. What is the most likely diagnosis in this case?

Options:
✓ Bezolds Abscess
  Citile abscess
  Lucs abscesses
  Mastoid abscesses
  Zygomatic abscesses

Explanation:
The description of an extracranial complication of chronic suppurative otitis media (CSOM) presenting as a swelling and pain along the sternocleidomastoid muscle, with bone erosion in the mastoid, is characteristic of a Bezold's abscess. This occurs when infection from the mastoid tip erodes through the bone and tracks into the neck.

Question 362 (KMC - 2023)

Question:
A 12 years old patient has presented to Emergency Room complaining of Fever & rigors for the last 4 days. He is also a diagnosed case of cholesteatoma left ear. Options:
  Acute mastoiditis
  Acute pyogenic meningitis
  Cerebellar abscess
✓ Lateral sinus thrombosis
  Otitic hydrocephalus

Explanation:
Cholesteatoma (a complication of CSOM) with persistent fever and rigors, along with mixed hearing loss and the use of MRV to assess venous flow, strongly points to lateral sinus thrombosis, a serious intracranial complication where a blood clot forms in the dural venous sinus due to adjacent infection.

Question 363 (KMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
An 11 years old boy was brought to emergency room by his mother complaining of unilateral sore throat, headache and difficulty in mouth opening for the last 7 days. On examination a palpable swelling is noticed at level-2 behind angle of the jaw and on oral cavity examination, swelling has extension posterior to the posterior pillar of the tonsil on same side. What is the most likely diagnosis in this case?

Options:
  Unilateral tonsillitis
  Peritonsillar abscesses
✓ Parapharyngeal abscesses
  Pleomorphic adenoma
  Retropharyngeal abscesses

Explanation:
The symptoms of unilateral sore throat, headache, difficulty in mouth opening (trismus), palpable swelling behind the angle of the jaw, and swelling extending posterior to the posterior pillar of the tonsil are classic for a parapharyngeal abscess. Peritonsillar abscesses typically deviate the tonsil medially and anteriorly.

Question 364 (KMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 55 year old man was brought to the emergency room complaining of hoarseness of voice for the last 7 months that is progressive and now having difficulty in breathing for the last three days. He is giving history of Carcinoma Larynx for which he received radiation. Flexible laryngoscopy shows fixed vocal cords with ulcerated growth and CT Scan shows heterogeneously enhancing lesion involving vocal cords and thyroid cartilage. What is the most appropriate T-stage classification for this patient?

Options:
  T0
  T1
  T2
  T3
✓ T4b

Explanation:
For laryngeal carcinoma, vocal cord fixation places the tumor at least at T3. Involvement of the thyroid cartilage or extension beyond the larynx classifies it as T4. T4b indicates invasion of the prevertebral space, encasement of the carotid artery, or invasion of mediastinal structures, signifying very advanced disease. Given the comprehensive involvement described, T4b is the most appropriate classification among the options, signifying extensive disease.

Question 365 (KMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 45 year old man has brought to the emergency room complaining of hoarseness of voice for the last 14 months that is progressive and now having difficulty in breathing for the last two days. He is giving history of Carcinoma Larynx for which he received radiation. The patient has developed decrease saturation for which intervention is needed. What is the best airway management option in this case?

Options:
  EndoTracheal Intubation
✓ Tracheostomy between ring two and three of trachea
  Low Tracheostomy
  Percutaneous Tracheostomy
  High Tracheostomy

Explanation:
In a patient with severe airway obstruction due to advanced laryngeal carcinoma (especially recurrent after radiation), with decreasing oxygen saturation, establishing a surgical airway is crucial. Tracheostomy between the second and third tracheal rings is the standard and safest site for this procedure, bypassing the obstruction in the larynx.

Question 366 (KMC - 2023)

Question:
A 60 year old man has presented to specialist outpatient complaining of painful swallowing, hoarseness of voice and sometimes regurgitation of food for the last 8 months. Examination of the neck and indirect laryngoscopy are unremarkable. His Hb level is 9.8g/dl, Total leucocyte count 15000mcl.   Laryngocele
  Oesopheageal carcinoma
✓ Pharyngeal pouch
  Piriform sinus carcinoma
  Postcricoid carcinoma

Explanation:
The symptoms of dysphagia, regurgitation of undigested food, and hoarseness (often due to aspiration), combined with a classic 'constant filling' on the side of the neck on barium swallow, are highly indicative of a pharyngeal pouch (Zenker's diverticulum).

Question 367 (KMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 5 year old patient is brought to consultant clinic by his parents complaining of pain in his right ear of sudden onset of three hours duration. Otoscopy shows red congested Tympanic membrane with bulge in the centre and the patient is febrile too. What is the most likely route of spread of infection in this case?

Options:
  Direct spread from external ear
  Haematogenous spread
  Infection of the Para Nasal Sinus is
  Patulous Eustachian Tube
✓ Shorter and wider Eustachian Tube s

Explanation:
The clinical picture describes acute otitis media (AOM) in a child. In children, AOM typically results from the spread of infection from the nasopharynx to the middle ear via the Eustachian tube. Their Eustachian tubes are anatomically shorter, wider, and more horizontal, making them more prone to dysfunction and infection.

Question:
A 55 years old patient has presented to outpatient department complaining of change in voice for the last 3 months that is persistent and progressive in nature.   Vocal cord polyp

Question 369 (KMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 19 years old patient was diagnosed as a case of Gradinigo syndrome due Chronic Supporative Otitis media 7 days ago. On examination, the postaural area is tender with scanty foul smelling discharge from the left ear. Pure tone audiometry shows mixed hearing loss. What is the most likely cause responsible for this condition?

Options:
  Acute mastoid abscess
✓ Acute petrositis
  Chronic otitis media.
  Lucus abscesses
  Secretory otitis media

Explanation:
Gradenigo's syndrome is a known complication of petrositis (inflammation of the petrous apex of the temporal bone), which in turn results from chronic suppurative otitis media. The triad of otitis media, pain in the trigeminal nerve distribution, and ipsilateral abducens nerve palsy defines Gradenigo's.

Question 370 (KMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 24 year old patient has history of foul smell discharge from right ear for the last 7 years. On examination he has attic defect and granulation tissue. For the last 7 days, he has developed a triad of ear discharge, retro-orbital pain and 6th nerve paralysis. Audiometry showed profound hearing loss. What is the most likely cause in this patient?

Options:
  Cavernous sinus thrombosis
  Lateral sinus thrombosis
  Labyrinthitis
  Mastoiditis
✓ Petrositis

Explanation:
The triad of chronic ear discharge (from CSOM/cholesteatoma), retro-orbital pain (trigeminal neuralgia), and 6th nerve (abducens) paralysis constitutes Gradenigo's syndrome, which is caused by inflammation of the petrous apex of the temporal bone, i.e., petrositis.

Question 371 (KMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 17-year-old patient has presented to outpatient complaining of right pain for the last 3 days. On otoscopic examination the tympanic membrane is red congested. The patient received oral antibiotics and nasal sprays but not relieved. Most effective treatment option in this patient to avoid complication is

Options:
  Intravenous Venous Antibiotics
  Nasal topical steroids
✓ Myringotomy with grommet insertion
  Tympanocentesis in anterior superior quadrant
  Tympanocentesis in anterior inferior quadrant

Explanation:
For persistent or refractory acute otitis media (AOM) with a red, bulging tympanic membrane, especially when pain is not relieved by antibiotics, myringotomy (incision of the tympanic membrane) with or without grommet insertion is an effective treatment. It relieves pressure, drains fluid, and can prevent complications like TM rupture or mastoiditis.

Question 372 (KMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 40-year-old man has presented to outpatient complaining of hoarseness of voice for the last 15 months persistent and progressive. Neck examination is normal and indirect laryngoscopy shows ulcerated growth on left vocal cord anterior one-third with normal movements. His flexible endoscopy shows same findings only white patch on right cord. What is the most likely T Stage of the disease in this case?

Options:
  Stage 1
  Stage 1a
✓ Stage 1b
  Stage 2a
  Stage 2b

Explanation:
For laryngeal carcinoma, an ulcerated growth on the vocal cord with normal vocal cord movements places it in T1. The presence of a lesion on one vocal cord (ulcerated growth on left) and a separate suspicious lesion on the contralateral cord (white patch on right) indicates involvement of both vocal cords, classifying it as T1b (tumor involves both vocal cords, but with normal mobility).

Question 373 (KMC - 2023)

Question:
A 9 year old patient has presented to outpatient department complaining of nasal obstruction and decreased hearing level in his both ears.   Mixed hearing loss
  Profound hearing loss
  Sensory Neural Hearing loss
  Sudden Sensory Neural Hearing loss

Explanation:
Enlarged adenoids can obstruct the Eustachian tubes, leading to middle ear effusion and a dull tympanic membrane with loss of landmarks. This results in a conductive hearing loss due to impaired sound transmission through the middle ear.

Question 374 (KMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 45 year old man has presented to outpatient department complaining of hoarseness of voice for the last 18 months that is persistent and progressive. He is farmer by profession. His neck examination is normal and indirect laryngoscopy shows ulcerated growth on right vocal cord anterior mid-portion with normal movements. His flexible endoscopy shows same findings, the patient is staged as T1aN0M0. Histopathology report shows Squamous Cell Carcinoma. After counseling for treatment, he is reluctant to go for Radiotherapy. What is the most likely next treatment option in this case?

Options:
  Chemotherapy
✓ Cordectomy
  Partial laryngectomy
  Subepithelial Cordectomy
  Total Laryngectomy

Explanation:
For T1a glottic squamous cell carcinoma, both radiotherapy and endoscopic laser cordectomy (a type of cordectomy) offer excellent cure rates with voice preservation. If the patient is reluctant for radiotherapy, surgical cordectomy (removal of the affected part of the vocal cord) is a standard alternative treatment option.

Question 376 (KMC - 2023)

Question:

Question 378 (KMC - 2023)

Question 379 (KMC - 2023)

Question:
A 45 year old male patient is presented to otolaryngology specialist clinic complaining of hoarseness of voice for the last 3 months. He Is chronic smoker and was using medicines for heartburn.   Hyperkeratosis

  Squamous cell carcinoma
  Verricous carcinoma

Persistent hoarseness with grayish-white patches on the vocal cords of a chronic smoker that cannot be easily excised are characteristic features of leukoplakia. Leukoplakia is a clinical term for a white patch that cannot be wiped off and is often a precancerous lesion, especially in smokers.

Question 380 (KMC - 2023)

Question:
A 17 year old girl was brought to the emergency room complaining of difficulty in breathing for two days. She also gives history of dental manipulation.   Pseudomonas aeruginosa
  Staphylococus aureus
  Streptococcus pneumonae
✓ Streptococcus viridans

Explanation:
The clinical presentation of difficulty breathing, protruded tongue with reduced movements, and a history of recent dental manipulation is highly suggestive of Ludwig's angina, a severe cellulitis of the floor of the mouth. This condition often results from odontogenic infections, and *Streptococcus viridans* (a common oral commensal) is a frequent causative organism.

Question 381 (KMC - 2023)

Question:
A 48 year old high school teacher has presented to outpatient complaining of hoarseness of voice for the last 16 months persistent and progressive. Question 382 (KMC - 2023)

Question 383 (KMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 7 year old girl was brought to toutpatient by her parents complaining of bilateral decreased hearing and nasal obstruction for the last 3 months. On otoscopy, she has bilateral dull tympanic membranes with loss of landmarks and is a mouth breather. Tympanometry reveals type B graph. What is the most effective treatment of choice in this case?

Options:
  Adeno Tonsillectomy
✓ Adenodectomy with Grometts insertion.
  Conservative treatment with analgesics
  Conservative treatment with antibiotics
  Myringotomy with ventilation, tube insertion

Explanation:
The combination of bilateral decreased hearing, nasal obstruction, mouth breathing, dull tympanic membranes, and a Type B tympanogram indicates otitis media with effusion (OME) secondary to enlarged adenoids. The most effective treatment is adenoidectomy (removal of the adenoids to address the obstruction) combined with myringotomy and grommet insertion (ventilation tubes) to drain the middle ear fluid and improve hearing.

Question 384 (KMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 5 year old boy is scheduled for diagnostic bronchoscopy. Clinical examination and XRay chest are normal. During rigid bronchoscopy, a piece of peanut was removed. In the recovery room the patient was not maintaining O2 saturation, with hyperresonant chest on percussion. What is the next step in the treatment of this patient?

Options:
  Endotracheal intubation
  Chest intubation
✓ ReBronchoscopy
  Tracheostomy
  Ventilator support

Explanation:
After removal of an airway foreign body, if the patient develops desaturation and hyperresonance on the chest, it raises concern for residual foreign body, airway edema causing persistent obstruction, or a complication like a pneumothorax (though hyperresonance usually suggests air trapping). Re-bronchoscopy would be the next step to re-evaluate the airway for any missed fragments, assess mucosal swelling, or rule out a post-extraction complication that might be causing ongoing obstruction/air trapping.

Question 385 (KMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 40 year old lady is coming to specialist clinic complaining of difficulty in swallowing for the last 2 years. She has history of total thyroidectomy for differentiated thyroid carcinoma 3 years back. Her Barium swallow is normal and flexible endoscopy shows right vocal cord paralysis. What is the most likely cause of dysphagia in this case?

Options:
✓ Decreased pharyngeal gradient pressure and decreased glottis closure
  Decreased pharyngeal gradient pressure and increased glottis closure
  Increased pharyngeal gradient pressure and increased glottis closure
  Increased pharyngeal gradient pressure and decreased glottis closure
  Superior constrictor muscle and cricothyroid muscle dysfunction

Explanation:
Unilateral vocal cord paralysis (a potential complication of thyroidectomy due to recurrent laryngeal nerve injury) primarily causes dysphagia by impairing glottic closure. This leads to inefficient protection of the airway during swallowing, causing aspiration and difficulty in bolus transport. While the exact phrasing of 'decreased pharyngeal gradient pressure' might be debated, the 'decreased glottis closure' is a direct and significant cause of dysphagia in this context.

Question 386 (KMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 20 year old boy presented to the Consultant Clinic complaining of sore throat and difficulty in swallowing for the last 06 months associated with fever body aches and pains. The patient is appearing in professional examination. On examination of the oral cavity the tongue having white reddish spots, multiple and tender to touch. What is the most likely clinical diagnosis in this case?

Options:
✓ Apthus ulcers
  Erythroplakia
  Leukoplakia
  Submucosal fibrosis
  Squamous cell carcinoma

Explanation:
The chronic nature of sore throat and difficulty swallowing, along with multiple, tender, whitish-reddish spots on the tongue, is most consistent with recurrent aphthous ulcers. While other chronic inflammatory conditions could present, aphthous ulcers are common, painful, and can recur over long periods.

Question 387 (KMC - 2023)

Question:
A 15 years old boy was brought to emergency room by his parents complaining of sore throat, headache and difficulty in opening mouth for the last threedays. What is the most probable diagnosis?

Options:
  Acute follicular tonsillitis
  Infectious mononucleosis
  Membranous tonsillitis
✓ Peritonsillar abscesses
  Parapharyngeal abscesses

Explanation:
The classic presentation of unilateral tonsil enlargement with medial deviation, uvula deviation to the opposite side, and trismus (difficulty opening mouth) in the context of sore throat and fever, is diagnostic of a peritonsillar abscess (quinsy).

Question 388 (KMC - 2023)

Question:
A 9 years old boy presented to consultant clinic for pain in the throat, fever, and severe body aches for the last 4 days. On examination, the oropharynx is congested with whitish layers and red spots, neck is also swollen and tender. What is the investigation of choice in this patient?

  ASOTitre
  Complete Blood Picture
  Culture and Sensitivity
  Paul Bunnel Test
✓ Rapid Antigen test

Explanation:
For acute pharyngitis with signs suggestive of streptococcal infection (whitish layers/exudates, congested oropharynx, tender neck nodes), a Rapid Antigen Detection Test (RADT) for Group A Streptococcus is the investigation of choice for immediate diagnosis, guiding early antibiotic treatment and preventing complications like rheumatic fever.

Question:
A 16 year old girl presented to the Consultant Clinic complaining of swelling in the floor of mouth for the last 06 months that is progressive.   Granuloma

✓ Ranula
  Submandibular duct stone

Question 390 (KMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 25 year old boy presented to emergency room complaining of burning sensation in the throat and difficulty in chewing food for the last 06 months associated with fever body aches and pains. The patient is addicted to Pan (Betel quid) and smoking. On examination of the oral cavity mobile mucosal surfaces of the tongue having white reddish spots, multiple and tender to touch. Which of the following is the most likely diagnosis in this case?

Options:
  Apthus ulcers
  Erythroplakia
  Leukoplakia
  Lichen planus
✓ Submucosal fibrosis

Explanation:
The long history of burning sensation, difficulty chewing, and the risk factors of betel quid chewing and smoking are strongly associated with oral submucous fibrosis (OSMF). OSMF is a chronic, progressive, precancerous condition characterized by blanching, stiffness, and eventual immobility of oral mucosal surfaces, leading to difficulty with chewing and swallowing. The 'white reddish spots' might represent associated inflammatory changes, but the overall picture with betel quid use points to OSMF.

Question 391 (KMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 45 year old man presented to outpatient complaining of hoarseness of voice for the last 12 months that is and progressive. Neck examination shows single ipsilateral lymph node less than 6cm, and indirect laryngoscopy shows ulcerated growth on the anterior one-third of the right vocal cord anterior one-third with normal movements. His flexible endoscopy shows same findings. What is the most likely TNM Stage of the disease in this case according to AGCC (American joint committee for cancer)?

Options:
✓ T1N1Mo
  T1N10Mo
  T1N20M1
  T1N10M2
  T₁N20M2

Explanation:
The ulcerated growth on the vocal cord with normal movements is T1 (confined to larynx, mobile cord). A single ipsilateral lymph node less than 6cm falls under N1 (single ipsilateral node <=3cm) or N2a (single ipsilateral node >3cm but <=6cm). Since the options given are in the format T1N1Mo, and the node is <6cm, N1 is the best fit, assuming it falls within the <=3cm category or simplified context, and with no distant metastasis (M0). The options with N10, N20, M1, M2 appear to be typos or highly unusual staging notations.

Question 392 (KMC - 2023)

Question:
A 9 years old boy was brought to emergency room by his mother complaining of sore throat, headache and severe body aches and pains. His both tonsils are covered by white follicles and on examination enlarged level-2 neck nodes on both sides. Which of the following is the most likely organisms which can cause for valvular heart disease in future?

  Group b haemolytic streptococci
  H-influenzae
  Staphylococus aureus
  Streptococcus viridans
✓ Streptococus pyogenes

Explanation:
*Streptococcus pyogenes* (Group A Streptococcus) is the causative agent of streptococcal pharyngitis (strep throat). Untreated or inadequately treated strep throat can lead to acute rheumatic fever (ARF), which is a major cause of acquired valvular heart disease in children.

Question:

Question:
A 42 year old man presented to the outpatient department complaining of difficulty in breathing and swallowing her the last 10 days that is progressive. Question 397 (KMC - 2023)

Question:
A 17-year-old boy was listed for tonsillectomy for repeated sore throat. On examination, the tonsil and enlarged size as grade-4. During surgical intervention, both tonsils were dissected while enlarged adenoids were left and not shaved. What is the most likely absolute contraindication for adenoids surgery in this case?

Options:
  Aberrant pharyngeal artery
  Bifid uvula
  Nasopharyngeal insufficiency
  Sinusitis
✓ Suspected Angiofibroma

Explanation:
Suspected angiofibroma is an absolute contraindication for routine adenoidectomy. Juvenile nasopharyngeal angiofibroma is a highly vascular tumor of the nasopharynx that can cause severe, life-threatening hemorrhage during surgery. If suspected, specialized surgical planning and preparation (e.g., embolization) are required.

  Neoadjunct radiotherapy
  Partial Laryngectomy
  Palliative care
✓ Total Laryngectomy

Explanation:
For advanced laryngeal carcinoma (fixed vocal cords, cartilage involvement, recurrent after radiation) with airway compromise, total laryngectomy is often the most appropriate definitive management option. Partial laryngectomy is unlikely to be sufficient, and palliative care is considered when curative options are exhausted or not feasible.

Question 406 (KMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 5 year old child was brought to the emergency room by her mother with complaints of difficulty in breathing and is unable to speak for the last 2 hours. The mother told that the kid was playing with toys On examination the child is in respiratory distress, and is cyanosed and exhausted. Her X-Ray chest is normal. What is the most likely diagnosis in this case?

Options:
✓ Acute Epiglottitis
  Acute Tracheobronchitis
  Foreign body Tracheobronchial tree
  Laryngitis
  Laryngomalacia

Explanation:
The acute onset of difficulty breathing, inability to speak (aphonia), respiratory distress, cyanosis, and exhaustion in a child is a life-threatening airway emergency. While playing with toys raises suspicion for a foreign body, acute epiglottitis (though less common now due to vaccination) presents with a rapidly progressing sore throat, dysphagia, drooling, and muffled or absent voice, leading to severe respiratory distress. A normal chest X-ray does not rule out upper airway obstruction.

Question 407 (KMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 15 years old girl was scheduled for tonsillectomy for repeated sore throat. On examination, the tonsils are enlarged in size with bilateral neck nodes. During surgery, the left tonsil has a bleeding vessel that bleeds repeatedly and a 2nd year resident is unable to control it. He has called for help of senior consultant. What is the most likely vessel that needs ligation in this case?

Options:
  Ascending Pharyngeal artery branch
✓ Branch of facial artery
  Descending Pharyngeal artery branch
  Lingual artery branch
  Paratonsillar vein

Explanation:
The tonsils are highly vascular. The main arterial supply to the palatine tonsils comes from the tonsillar branch of the facial artery. Persistent, uncontrolled bleeding during tonsillectomy often necessitates ligation of this or other major branches of the external carotid artery system that supply the tonsil, with the facial artery being a key source.

Question 408 (KMC - 2023)

Question:
A 50 year old man was to the brought to the emergency room complaining of difficulty in breathing for the last three days. He is uncontrolled diabetic, that and also giving history dental manipulation by Dentist. On examination the tongue is protruded with sublingual swelling. What is the most appropriate clinical diagnosis?

  Parapharyngeal abscesses.
  Retropharyngeal abscesses
  Submandibular duct stone
  Submandibular abscesses

Explanation:
The rapidly progressive difficulty breathing, protruded tongue with sublingual swelling, and a history of recent dental manipulation in an uncontrolled diabetic (who is immunocompromised) are classic signs of Ludwig's angina. This is a severe, rapidly spreading cellulitis of the floor of the mouth that can quickly compromise the airway.

Question 409 (KMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
An 18 year old boy presents to Emergency department with history of severe headache which he claims to be the worst of his life. Headache is associated with vomiting and neck pain. Patient is giving history of nasal obstruction and post-nasal drip. On examination, there is mucopus in both nostrils. What specific investigation you would like to order immediately?

Options:
  CSF analysis
✓ CT Brain & PNS with contrast
  CT Brain without contrast
  MRI Brain
  Pet scan

Explanation:
The 'worst headache of his life' with meningeal signs (neck pain, vomiting) is a red flag for subarachnoid hemorrhage (SAH), which primarily requires an unenhanced CT brain. However, the co-existing nasal obstruction, post-nasal drip, and mucopus suggest sinusitis, which can lead to intracranial complications (e.g., meningitis, abscess). A CT scan of the brain with contrast, combined with a CT of the paranasal sinuses, would provide a comprehensive evaluation for both potential causes of severe headache and any associated complications.

Question 410 (KMC - 2023)

Question:
A 39 year old lady presented to the Emergency room with profuse epistaxis for 4 hours. He has similar episodes in the past. Anterior nasal packing was done in emergency room but bleeding was uncontrolled. All baseline investigations were normal. The consultant decided to control the bleeding under General Anesthesia.   Septal artery

Profuse epistaxis refractory to anterior nasal packing typically indicates a posterior bleed. The sphenopalatine artery (often misspelled in options as Spenopalatine artery) is the primary arterial supply to the posterior nasal cavity and is the most common target for endoscopic ligation to control severe posterior epistaxis.

Question 411 (KMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A middle age lady presented to outpatients department with complaints of nasal obstruction, headache and postnasal discharge for the last 9 months. Clinical examination, shows unilateral polyp, mucopus and mucin, CT scan shows double density opacity in the nose, Maxillary and Ethmoid region pushing the orbit on same side. What is the most appropriate treatment option in this case?

Options:
  Endoscopic sinus surgery
  Endoscopic sinus surgery plus topical nasal steroi ds.
✓ Endoscopic sinus surgery plus topical and oral steroi ds
  IV Amphoteracine B
  Topical nasal steroi ds

Explanation:
The constellation of chronic unilateral nasal obstruction, headache, mucopus/mucin, unilateral polyp, and the classic 'double-density opacity' on CT (indicating fungal concretions within allergic mucin) that is pushing the orbit, is diagnostic of Allergic Fungal Rhinosinusitis (AFS). The most appropriate treatment for AFS involves endoscopic sinus surgery for debridement, followed by a prolonged course of topical and oral corticosteroids to manage the allergic inflammation and prevent recurrence.

Question:
A postoperative thyroidectomy patient has noticed change in voice while singing with normal conversation for the last 40 days. Most likely external branch of superior laryngeal nerve has injured during surgery 2 months back. Most likely muscle paralyzed in this case is

  C. Posterior Cricoarytenoid
  D. Thyroarytenoid
  E. Vocalis

Question 424 (KMC - 2025)

Question:
A 15 years old male patient presents to emergency room with complaints of sore throat, Painful swallowing and high grade fever for the last 3 days. Patient has difficulty in breathing as well. X ray Neck lateral view shows thumb sign. Most likely clinical diagnosis in this case is

Options:
✓ A. Epiglottitis
  B. Laryngeal Malignancy
  C. Laryngotracheobronchitis
  D. Peritonsillar Abscess
  E. Retropharyngeal abscess

Explanation:
The 'Thumb sign' on a lateral neck X-ray is the pathognomonic radiological finding for acute Epiglottitis (swollen epiglottis).

Question 425 (KMC - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
A 9 years old female patient presents to emergency room with complaints of sore throat, Painful swallowing and difficulty in breathing for the last 4 days. She is sitting in tripod position and cannot lying down for sleep. The most important step in examination that should be avoided

Options:
  A. Indirect laryngoscopy
  B. Laryngeal crepitus
  C. Nasal cavity examination
✓ D. Oral cavity examination
  E. Posterior rhinoscopy

Explanation:
In suspected Acute Epiglottitis, examination of the oral cavity (e.g., using a tongue depressor) is contraindicated as it may precipitate laryngospasm and complete airway obstruction.

Question 426 (KMC - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
A 45 years old male patient presents to OPD with complaints of change in his voice for the last 2 years. There is history of voice abuse and the patient is also a chronic smoker. Indirect laryngoscopy shows a vocal cord polyp. Most appropriate treatment option for this patient is

Options:
  A. LASER
✓ B. Microlaryngoscopic surgery
  C. Reassurance
  D. Speech therapy
  E. Voice Rest

Explanation:
Vocal cord polyps typically require surgical excision (microlaryngoscopy) because, unlike nodules, they rarely resolve with conservative management like speech therapy or voice rest alone.

Question 427 (KMC - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
A 30 year old lady operated by Neurosurgeon for brain tumour and was put on ventilator for the last 4 days. The ICU team decided tracheostomy by an experienced ENT surgeon. Most suitable tracheal rings for tracheostomy are in this patient

Options:
  A. Cricoid and thyroid cartilage
  B. Fifth and sixth rings
  C. First and Second rings
✓ D. Second and third rings
  E. Third and fourth rings

Explanation:
The standard site for an elective tracheostomy is between the 2nd and 3rd (or 3rd and 4th) tracheal rings to avoid subglottic stenosis (high) or vessel injury (low).

Question 428 (KMC - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
A high school teacher of 39 years old has neck swelling for the last 3 years; she is scheduled for thyroid surgery. Most common location to identify and preserve recurrent laryngeal nerve during Thyroid surgery is

Options:
  A. Lateral to the carotid artery
  B. Lateral to the inferior thyroid artery
  C. Medial to the carotid
  D. Medial to the inferior thyroid artery
✓ E. Tracheo esophageal groove

Explanation:
The recurrent laryngeal nerve ascends in the tracheoesophageal groove, which is the primary anatomical landmark for its identification and preservation during thyroidectomy.

Question 429 (KMC - 2025)

Question:
A 19 years old patient was diagnosed as a case of Gradinigo syndrome due Chronic Suppurative Otitis Media 7 days ago. On examination, the post aural area is tender with scanty foul smelling discharge from the ear. Pure tone audiometry shows mixed hearing loss. What is the most likely cause responsible for this condition?

Options:
  A. Acute mastoid abscess
✓ B. Acute petrositis
  C. Chronic otitis media
  D. Lucus abscesses
  E. Secretory otitis media

Explanation:
Gradenigo's syndrome (triad of otorrhea, retro-orbital pain, and abducens nerve palsy) is the classic presentation of Acute Petrositis (inflammation of the petrous apex).

Question 430 (KMC - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
A diagnosed case of chronic otitis media has presented to Emergency Room complaining of ear swelling for the 7 days. Otoscopic examination shows choleasteatoma in attic region of left ear. Pure Tone Audiometry shows Air Bone Gap of 10 db, and CT Scan shows opacity in the antrum. Most like management in this case is

Options:
  A. Cortical mastidectomy
  B. Conservative Management
✓ C. Modified Radical Mastidectomy
  D. Mastoid exploration
  E. Radical Mastidectomy

Explanation:
Cholesteatoma requires surgical removal. A Modified Radical Mastoidectomy is the procedure of choice to remove the disease from the mastoid and attic while preserving the hearing mechanism.

Question 431 (KMC - 2025)

Question:
A 9 year old patient has presented to outpatient department complaining of nasal obstruction and decreased hearing level in his both ears. Examination shows high arched palate and dull tympanic membrane with loss of landmarks. X Ray nasopharynx shows enlarge Adenoids. What is the most likely cause of hearing loss in this case?

Options:
✓ A. Conductive hearing loss
  B. Mixed hearing loss
  C. Profound hearing loss
  D. Sensory Neural Hearing loss
  E. Sudden Sensory Neural Hearing loss

Explanation:
Adenoid hypertrophy obstructs the Eustachian tube, causing Secretory Otitis Media (Glue Ear), which results in Conductive Hearing Loss.

Question:
A 16 year old girl presented to the Consultant Clinic complaining of swelling in the floor of mouth for the last 06 months that is progressive. Options:
  A. Dermoid cyst
  B. Granuloma
  C. Papilloma
✓ D. Ranula
  E. Submandibular duct stone

Explanation:
A Ranula is a retention cyst of the sublingual gland, characterized clinically as a soft, bluish, translucent swelling in the floor of the mouth.

Question 435 (KMC - 2025)

Question:

For the last 7 days, he has developed a triad of ear discharge, retro-orbital pain and 6th nerve paralysis. Audiometry showed profound hearing loss. What is the most likely cause in this patient?

Options:
  A. Cavernous sinus thrombosis
  B. Lateral sinus thrombosis
  C. Labyrinthitis
  D. Mastoiditis
✓ E. Petrositis

Explanation:

Question:

  B. Oesophageal carcinoma
✓ C. Pharyngeal pouch
  D. Piriform sinus carcinoma
  E. Post cricoid carcinoma

Explanation:
Regurgitation of food and a 'filling' (pouch) visible on barium swallow on the side of the neck are findings consistent with a Pharyngeal Pouch (Zenker's Diverticulum).

  A. Group b haemolytic streptococci
  B. H-influenzae
  C. Staphylococcus aureus
  D. Streptococcus viridans
✓ E. Streptococcus pyogenes

Explanation:
Streptococcus pyogenes (Group A Beta-hemolytic Streptococcus) causes acute tonsillitis and can lead to Rheumatic Heart Disease (valvular disease) as a post-infectious sequela.

Question 439 (KMC - 2025)

Question:
A 5 year old boy is scheduled for diagnostic bronchoscopy. Clinical examination and X Ray chest are normal. During rigid bronchoscopy, a piece of peanut was removed. In the recovery room the patient was not maintaining O2 saturation, with hyper resonant chest on percussion. What is the next step in the treatment of this patient?

Options:
  A. Endotracheal intubation
✓ B. Chest intubation
  C. Re Bronchoscopy
  D. Tracheostomy
  E. Ventilator support

Explanation:
Hyper-resonance and desaturation following bronchoscopy strongly suggest a Pneumothorax (a complication of the procedure). The treatment is chest tube insertion (referred to here as chest intubation).

Question 440 (KMC - 2025)

Question:
A 45 year old man has brought to the emergency room complaining of hoarseness of voice for the last 14 months that is progressive and now having difficulty in breathing for the last two days. Options:
  A. Endo Tracheal Intubation
  B. High Tracheostomy
  C. Low Tracheostomy
  D. Per cutaneous Tracheostomy
✓ E. Tracheostomy between ring two and three of trachea

Explanation:
In a patient with laryngeal carcinoma and airway compromise, tracheostomy is the preferred airway management. The standard placement is between the 2nd and 3rd tracheal rings.

Question 441 (KMC - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
40-year-old hypertensive man presented to emergency department with history of sudden onset severe epistaxis. After proper history, examination and investigation anterior nasal packing was done but unsuccessful. You are called for vessel ligation under general anesthesia through endoscope. What is the most appropriate vessel to be ligated through endoscope to stop bleeding?

Options:
  A. Anterior ethmoidal artery
  B. Posterior ethmoidal artery
✓ C. Septal branch of sphenopalatine artery
  D. Septal branch of greater palatine artery
  E. Septal branch of superior labial artery

Explanation:
The Sphenopalatine artery is the primary vessel responsible for severe posterior epistaxis. Endoscopic ligation of the Sphenopalatine artery (or its branches) is the standard treatment for intractable bleeding.

Question 442 (KMC - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
A 39 year old lady presented to the Emergency room with profuse epistaxis for 4 hours. He has similar episodes in the past. Anterior nasal packing was done in emergency room but bleeding was uncontrolled. All base line investigations were normal. The consultant decided to control the bleeding under General Anesthesia. What is the most likely vessel that need to be clipped with Functional Endoscope in this case?

Options:
  A. Anterior Ethmoidal
  B. Greater palatine artery
  C. Posterior Ethmoidal
✓ D. Spenopalatine artery
  E. Septal artery

Explanation:
Uncontrolled epistaxis (especially when anterior packing fails) typically originates from the posterior nasal cavity, supplied mainly by the Sphenopalatine artery, which is the target for clipping/ligation.

Question 443 (KMC - 2025)

Question:
A 17 year old patient has presented to outpatient complaining of right pain for the last 3 days. On otoscopic examination the tympanic membrane is red congested. The patient received oral antibiotics and nasal sprays but not relived. Most effective treatment option in this patient to avoid complication is

Options:
  A. Intravenous Venous Antibiotics
  B. Nasal topical steroids
  C. Myringotomy with grommet insertion
  D. Tympanocentesis in anterior superior quadrant
✓ E. Tympanocentesis in anterior inferior quadrant

Explanation:
In severe acute otitis media unresponsive to medication, tympanocentesis (or myringotomy) is performed in the anterior inferior quadrant to drain the middle ear and prevent complications.

Question 444 (KMC - 2025)

Question:
A 9 years old boy has presented to consultant outpatient complaining of bilateral decreased hearing level for the last 2 months. Otoscopy shows dull tympanic membrane and Tympanometry reveals type B tympanogram. What is the most likely diagnosis in this case?

Options:
  A. Acute suppurative otitis media
  B. Chronic otitis media
  C. Retracted tympanic membrane
✓ D. Secretory otitis media
  E. Tympanosclerosis

Explanation:
A dull tympanic membrane combined with a Type B (flat) tympanogram indicates fluid in the middle ear, characteristic of Secretory Otitis Media (Otitis Media with Effusion).

Question:
A 27 year old patient is complaining of nasal obstruction for the last 7 months that is persistent and progressive and is associated with headache. He has no history of any nasal surgery.   A. Antibiotics first line
✓ B. FESS
  C. Intra nasal polypectomy
  D. Topical and oral steroids
  E. Topical Xylometazoline

Explanation:
The clinical presentation is consistent with nasal polyposis. Functional Endoscopic Sinus Surgery (FESS) is the standard surgical treatment to clear the sinuses and remove polyps.

Question 446 (KMC - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
A 4 year old patient comes to consultant clinic with complaints of mouth breathing, snoring during sleep and decreased hearing level in both ears for the last 15 days. X Ray nasopharynx shows enlarged adenoid. What is the next most likely investigation to help in diagnosis?

Options:
  A. BERA (Brain Stem Evoked Response Audiometry)
  B. Otoacoustic emissions
  C. Pure Tone Audiometry
✓ D. Pure Tone Audiometry and Tympanometry
  E. Tympanometry

Explanation:
To assess the impact of adenoid hypertrophy on the ears (specifically Secretory Otitis Media), both Pure Tone Audiometry (for hearing threshold) and Tympanometry (for middle ear pressure) are indicated.

Question 447 (KMC - 2025)

Question:
You received a consultation call of a 2 year old patient who is in severe respiratory distress for the last 2 days. He is not maintaining saturation. On examination, the patient is in inspiratory distress with intercostal recession and X-Ray shows steeple sign. Saturation is decreasing and the patient develops peripheral cyanosis. What is the most suitable management option in this case?

Options:
  A. Bronchoscopy
  B. Cricothyrodoctomy
✓ C. Endotracheal intubation
  D. Mask oxygenation with helium
  E. Tracheostomy

Explanation:
The 'Steeple sign' indicates Croup. In severe cases with cyanosis and respiratory failure, immediate airway control via Endotracheal Intubation is required.

Question 448 (KMC - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
A 34 years old lady is listed for total thyroidectomy for huge thyroid swelling; the swelling is for the last [..] years. After completion of surgery the surgeon noticed tracheomalacia, most common cause of respiratory obstruction upon extubation. Most likely intervention in this case is

Options:
  A. Mask oxygenation
  B. Cricothyroidectomy
  C. Stitch trachea to the skin
✓ D. Tracheostomy
  E. Ventilator support

Explanation:
Tracheomalacia involves the collapse of the tracheal wall due to loss of structural integrity (often from prolonged compression by a goiter). A tracheostomy is the definitive management to secure the airway by bypassing the collapsed segment.

Question 449 (KMC - 2025)

Question:
A 37 years old lady has presented to out patients department complaining of nasal obstruction, headache and post nasal drip mucoid in nature for the last 7 years. CT scan shows hyperdense opacity in the Ethmoid region more close to the orbit. She has history of NSAIDS, Antibiotics and nasal sprays. Most likely diagnosis in this case is

Options:
  A. Allergic Rhinitis
✓ B. Allergic Fungal Sinusitis
  C. Invasive Fungal Sinusitis
  D. Maxillary Sinusitis
  E. Osteoma Ethmoid Sinus

Explanation:
The presence of hyperdense opacities (fungal mucin) on CT, along with a chronic history and implied atopy/sensitivity (NSAIDS history often correlates with Samter's triad/polyps), is characteristic of Allergic Fungal Sinusitis.

  D. Invertid papilloma
  E. Maxillary sinusitis

The "double density" sign on a CT scan (central hyperdensity with peripheral hypodensity) combined with allergic mucin is pathognomonic for Allergic Fungal Sinusitis.

  A. Cordectomy segmental
  B. Chemoradiotherapy
✓ C. Radiotherapy
  D. Subepithelial Cordectomy
  E. Total Laryngectomy

Explanation:
For a voice professional (teacher) with early-stage (T1a) glottic carcinoma, Radiotherapy is often the preferred treatment modality to preserve the best possible voice quality compared to surgery.

Question 452 (KMC - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
A 42 year old man presented to the outpatient department complaining of difficulty in breathing and swallowing for the last 10 days that is progressive. Oral examination shows swelling in the oropharynx on right side and the patient is well oriented. X ray neck lateral view in extension shows increased shadow of the retro pharynx with erosion of the corresponding vertebrae. What is the most appropriate treatment in this case?

Options:
✓ A. Anti-Tubercular Therapy
  B. Incision and drainage through cervical incision
  C. IV Antibiotics
  D. Per oral incision and drainage
  E. Tracheostomy

Explanation:
Retropharyngeal abscess with erosion of the cervical vertebrae is highly suggestive of Pott's disease (Tuberculosis of the spine) presenting as a cold abscess. The primary treatment is Anti-Tubercular Therapy (ATT).

Question 453 (KMC - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
A 16 year old boy has presented to Emergency Room with sudden onset of headache and vomiting. He has also history of foul smell right ear discharge for the last 6 years. On examination, there is scanty discharge and granulation tissue in the attic region of right ear and difficulty in closing the right eye. What is the most likely Extra cranial complication of Chronic Suppurative Otitis Media in this case?

Options:
  A. Brain abscess
  B. Epidural abscess
✓ C. Facial nerve paralysis
  D. Lateral sinus thrombosis
  E. Otitis hydrocephalus

Explanation:
Facial nerve paralysis (intratemporal) is classified as an extracranial complication (or intratemporal complication), whereas brain abscess, epidural abscess, lateral sinus thrombosis, and otitic hydrocephalus are intracranial complications.

  D. Maxillary sinusitis
  E. Osteoma Eethmoid Sinus

  C. Descending Pharyngeal artery branch
  D. Lingual artery branch
  E. Paratonsillar vein

Question 460 (KMC - 2025)

Question:
[..] year old man was brought to the emergency room complaining of hoarseness of voice for the last 7 months that is progressive and now having difficulty in breathing for the last three days. He is giving history of Carcinoma Larynx for which he received radiation. Flexible laryngoscopy shows fixed vocal cords with ulcerated growth and CT Scan shows heterogeneously enhancing lesion involving vocal cords and thyroid cartilage. What is the most appropriate T-stage classification for this patient?

Options:
  A. T0
  B. T1
  C. T2
  D. T3
✓ E. T4a

Explanation:
Tumor invasion into the thyroid cartilage (usually indicated by heterogeneous enhancement involving the cartilage) classifies the laryngeal cancer as T4a. Vocal cord fixation alone would be T3.

Question 461 (KMC - 2025)

Question:
A 7 years old boy was brought to emergency room by his mother complaining of sore throat, headache and difficulty in breathing. His both tonsils are covered by grey, white membrane; red spots on the soft palate and periorbital edema are noted. The neck has enlarged tender lymph nodes on both sides. His lymphocyte/WBC ratio is higher than 0.35. What is the most likely diagnosis?

Options:
  A. Acute membranous tonsillitis
  B. Acute follicular tonsillitis
  C. Candidiasis
  D. Diphtheria
✓ E. Infectious mononucleosis

Explanation:
The combination of membranous tonsillitis, palatal petechiae (red spots), periorbital edema (Hoagland sign), and lymphocytosis is diagnostic of Infectious Mononucleosis (Glandular Fever).

  E. T4b N0 M0

Question 463 (KMC - 2025)

Question:
An 11 years old boy was brought to emergency room by his mother complaining of unilateral sore throat, headache and difficulty in mouth opening for the last 7 days. On examination a palpable swelling is noticed at level-2 behind angle of the jaw and on oral cavity examination, swelling has extension posterior to the posterior pillar of the tonsil on same side.   B. Peri tonsillar abscesses
✓ C. Para pharyngeal abscesses
  D. Pleomorphic adenoma
  E. Retro pharyngeal abscesses

Question 464 (KMC - 2025)

  D. Ligation of blood vessel under general anesthesia
  E. Packing of the tonsil bed with Adrenaline soaked

Question 465 (KMC - 2025)

Question:
A 45-year-old man presented to outpatient department with complaints of nasal obstruction, headache and post nasal drip mucoid green in color for the last 3 days. On examination, there is mucopus in the left nostril and X PNS shows a hazy sinus on ipsilateral side. What is the most appropriate investigation?

Options:
  A. Complete blood picture
✓ B. CT nose and PNS
  C. Culture and sensitivity
  D. Functional Endoscopic Sinus
  E. Histopathology of sinus mucosa

Explanation:
While acute rhinosinusitis is often diagnosed clinically, if an investigation is required to confirm sinus pathology or anatomy (especially with green mucopus/hazy X-ray prompting further look), CT Nose and PNS is the gold standard imaging modality.

Question 466 (KMC - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
A 47 year old man was brought to the emergency room complaining of hoarseness of voice for the last 12 months persistent and progressive and now difficulty in breathing for the last two days. He is giving history of Carcinoma Larynx for which he received radiation. Flexible laryngoscopy shows fixed vocal cords with ulcerated growth and CT Scan shows heterogeneously enhancing lesion involving vocal cords and thyroid cartilage sparing pharyngeal mucosa. What is most management option in this case?

Options:
  A. Chemotherapy
  B. Neo adjunct radiotherapy
  C. Partial Laryngectomy
  D. Palliative care
✓ E. Total Laryngectomy

Explanation:
For recurrent laryngeal carcinoma (post-radiation failure) with cartilage invasion (T4a) and airway compromise, salvage Total Laryngectomy is the standard curative treatment.

Question 467 (KMC - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
A 40 year old man has presented to outpatient department complaining of hoarseness of voice for the last few months that is persistent and progressive. He is farmer by profession. His neck examination is normal. Indirect laryngoscopy shows ulcerated growth on right vocal cord anterior mid portion with normal movements. His flexible endoscopy shows same findings, the patient is staged as T1a. Pathology report shows Squamous Cell Carcinoma. After counseling for treatment he is reluctant for radiotherapy. What is the most likely next treatment option in this case?

Options:
  A. Chemotherapy
✓ B. Cordectomy
  C. Partial laryngectomy
  D. Sub epithelial Cordectomy
  E. Total Laryngectomy

Explanation:
For T1a glottic carcinoma (tumor limited to one vocal cord with normal mobility), the standard treatment options are radiotherapy or surgery. If the patient refuses radiotherapy, the surgical choice is Cordectomy (often transoral laser microsurgery).

Question 468 (KMC - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
A middle aged male patient is scheduled for nasal surgery for Ethmoidal polypi. Endoscopic examination shows no landmarks which suggest previous surgery. Further examination shows mucopus and debris. CT Scan also shows heterogenous opacities in all sinuses. What is the first surgical step in this case?

Options:
  A. Antrostomy
  B. Inferior Turbinectomy
  C. Middle Turbinectomy
  D. Polypectomy
✓ E. Unsinectomy

Explanation:
In functional endoscopic sinus surgery (FESS), especially when landmarks are distorted or lost due to previous surgery or disease, identifying the uncinate process and performing an Uncinectomy is the critical first step to identify the maxillary ostium and establish anatomical orientation.

Question:
12 year old boy has come to Outpatient clinic complaining of discharge from his right ear for the last few days.   A. H. influenzae
  B. Pneumococcus
✓ C. Pseudomonas Aurgenosa
  D. Staphylococcus
  E. Streptococcus

Explanation:
The clinical picture describes Chronic Suppurative Otitis Media (CSOM) of the tubotympanic (mucosal) type. Pseudomonas aeruginosa is the most common organism isolated in CSOM.

✓ A. Apthus ulcers
  B. Erythroplakia
  C. Leukoplakia
  D. Submucosal fibrosis
  E. Squamous cell carcinoma

Explanation:
Multiple, tender ulcers on the mobile mucosa, often triggered by stress (professional exams), are characteristic of Recurrent Aphthous Stomatitis (Aphthous ulcers).

Question 472 (KMC - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
A 40 year old lady is coming to specialist clinic complaining of difficulty in swallowing for the last 2 years. She has history of total thyroidectomy for differentiated thyroid carcinoma 3 years back. Her Barium swallow is normal and flexible endoscopy shows right vocal cord paralysis. What is the most likely cause of dysphagia in this case?

Options:
✓ A. Decreased pharyngeal gradient pressure and decreased glottis closure
  B. Decreased pharyngeal gradient pressure and increased glottis closure
  C. Increased pharyngeal gradient pressure and increased glottis closure
  D. Increased pharyngeal gradient pressure and decreased glottis closure
  E. Superior constrictor muscle and cricothyroid muscle dysfunction

Explanation:
Vocal cord paralysis leads to glottic incompetence (decreased glottic closure). This inability to close the glottis tightly impairs the generation of subglottic pressure and disrupts the pharyngeal pressure gradient required for effective swallowing.

Question 473 (KMC - 2025)

Question:
A 40 year old man has presented to outpatient complaining of hoarseness of voice for the last 15 months persistent and progressive. Neck examination is normal and indirect laryngoscopy shows ulcerated growth on left vocal cord anterior one third with normal movements. His flexible endoscopy shows same findings only white patch on right cord. What is the most likely T Stage of the disease in this case?

Options:
  A. Stage 1
  B. Stage 1a
✓ C. Stage 1b
  D. Stage 2a
  E. Stage 2b

Explanation:
Tumor involving the left vocal cord plus a white patch (lesion) on the right vocal cord signifies involvement of both vocal cords. Since mobility is normal, this is classified as T1b (Stage 1b in the context of the options provided).

Question 474 (KMC - 2025)
================================================================================

Question:
A 15 years old patient has presented to a consultant clinic for complaining of right ear discharge, swelling and pain for last 20 days. The consultant notices a swelling and labeled it as an extra cranial complication of choronic supportive otitis media along the sterocleidomastoid muscle. CT Scan shows erosion of the bone at sinudural angle. What is the most likely diagnosis in this case?

Options:
✓ A. Bezolds Abscess
  B. Citile abscess
  C. Lucs abscesses
  D. Mastoid abscesses
  E. Zygomatic abscesses

Explanation:
A Bezold's abscess is a complication of mastoiditis where pus breaks through the mastoid tip and tracks down into the neck along the sternocleidomastoid muscle.

Question 475 (KMC - 2025)

Question:

According to the mother, the feeding and sleep of the baby is adequate. On examination, the baby is not cyanosed and is having mild chest recession. Options:
  A. Choanal atresia
  B. Epiglottitis
✓ C. Laryngomalacia
  D. Laryngotracheobronchitis
  E. Vocal cord Web

Explanation:
Laryngomalacia is the most common cause of congenital stridor, characterized by short aryepiglottic folds and an omega-shaped epiglottis, typically presenting shortly after birth.

  b. Acute pyogenic meningitis
  c. Cerebellar abscess
✓ d. Lateral sinus thrombosis
  e. Otic hydrocephalus

Options:
  a. Direct spread from external ear
  b. Haematogenous spread
  c. Infection of the Para Nasal Sinus
  d. Patulous Eustachian Tube
✓ e. Shorter and wider Eustachian Tube

Explanation:
Acute Otitis Media is more common in children because their Eustachian tubes are shorter, wider, and more horizontal, facilitating reflux of nasopharyngeal pathogens.

  c. Chronic otitis media
  d. Luc's abscess
  e. Secretory otitis media

Explanation:
Gradenigo's syndrome (otorrhea, retro-orbital pain, VI nerve palsy) is the hallmark of Petrositis (inflammation of the petrous apex).

Question:

  c. Labyrinthitis
  d. Luc's abscess
✓ e. Petrositis

The triad of otorrhea, retro-orbital pain, and abducens (6th) nerve palsy constitutes Gradenigo's triad, which indicates Petrositis.

Question 482 (KMC - 2024)

Question:
A 17-year-old patient has presented to outpatient complaining of right ear pain for the last 3 days. On otoscopic examination, the tympanic membrane is red and congested. The patient received oral antibiotics and nasal sprays but is not relieved. What is the most effective treatment option in this patient to avoid complications?

Options:
  a. Intravenous Venous Antibiotics
  b. Nasal topical steroids
  c. Myringotomy with grommet insertion
  d. Tympanocentesis in anterior superior quadrant
✓ e. Tympanocentesis in anterior inferior quadrant

Explanation:
In severe Acute Otitis Media unresponsive to antibiotics with a bulging TM, drainage is required. Tympanocentesis (or Myringotomy) is indicated. The anterior inferior quadrant is a safe site to avoid ossicles.

Question:

What is the most likely extra-cranial complication of Chronic Suppurative Otitis Media in this case?

Options:
  a. Brain abscess
  b. Epidural abscess
✓ c. Facial nerve paralysis
  d. Otitis hydrocephalus
  e. Petrositis

Explanation:
Difficulty in closing the eye indicates Facial Nerve Paralysis. Among the options, this is the only one typically classified as an intratemporal/extracranial complication, whereas the others are intracranial (except Petrositis which doesn't cause this specific sign directly).

Question:
A 9-year-old boy was brought to the emergency room by his mother complaining of sore throat, headache, and severe body aches and pains. His both tonsils are covered by white follicles, and on examination, enlarged level-2 neck nodes on both sides are noted. Which of the following is the most likely organism which can cause valvular heart disease in future?

Options:
  a. Group B haemolytic streptococci
  b. H. influenzae
  c. Staphylococcus aureus
  d. Streptococcus viridans
✓ e. Streptococcus pyogenes

Explanation:
Streptococcus pyogenes (Group A Beta-hemolytic Streptococcus) is the causative agent of Rheumatic Fever, which can lead to valvular heart disease.

Question:

His both tonsils are covered by grey, white membrane; red spots on the soft palate and periorbital edema are noted. The neck has enlarged tender lymph nodes on both sides. Options:
  a. Acute membranous tonsillitis
  b. Acute follicular tonsillitis
  c. Candidiasis
  d. Diphtheria
✓ e. Infectious mononucleosis

Explanation:
The clinical picture of membrane, palatal petechiae, periorbital edema, and lymphocytosis is characteristic of Infectious Mononucleosis.

Question:

Oral examination shows swelling in the oropharynx on the right side, and the mass seems to be well-oriented. X-ray neck lateral view in extension shows a prominent shadow at the retropharynx with erosion of the corresponding vertebrae. What is the most appropriate treatment in this case?

Options:
✓ a. Anti-Tubercular Therapy
  b. Incision and drainage through cervical incision
  c. Antibiotics
  d. Per oral incision and drainage
  e. Tracheostomy

Explanation:
Retropharyngeal abscess with vertebral erosion suggests Tuberculosis (Pott's disease/Cold abscess). The primary treatment is Anti-Tubercular Therapy.

Question:
A 17-year-old boy was listed for tonsillectomy for repeated sore throat. On examination, the tonsils are graded in size as grade-4. During surgical intervention, both tonsils were dissected while enlarged adenoids were left and not shaved. What is the most likely absolute contraindication for adenoid surgery in this case?

Options:
  a. Aberrant pharyngeal artery
✓ b. Bifid uvula
  c. Nasopharyngeal insufficiency
  d. Sinusitis
  e. Suspected Angiofibroma

Explanation:
A bifid uvula indicates a submucous cleft palate. Adenoidectomy in such cases can lead to Velopharyngeal Insufficiency.

Question 509 (KMC - 2024)

Question:
A 15-year-old boy presented to the emergency room spitting repeated blood-stained saliva from the oral cavity. He has a history of tonsillectomy 9 days back. On examination, the right tonsil fossa has a clot with no white slough. The patient is hemodynamically stable but pale and tries to vomit; saturation is 96% and Hb 9g/dl with raised TLC count. What is the most appropriate next step in management?

Options:
  a. Blood transfusions
  b. Crystalloids infusions
✓ c. IV Antibiotics
  d. Ligation of blood vessel under general anesthesia
  e. Packing of the tonsil bed with Adrenaline-soaked gauze

Explanation:
Secondary hemorrhage (due to infection) is typically managed by admission and IV antibiotics. Surgical intervention is reserved for uncontrolled bleeding.

Question:

Options:
✓ a. Amoxicillin
  b. Brufen
  c. Clarithromycin
  d. Cefadroxil
  e. Cefaclor

Explanation:
Amoxicillin often causes a maculopapular rash and can exacerbate symptoms in patients with Infectious Mononucleosis.

  b. Chronic tonsillitis
✓ c. Malignancy
  d. Peritonsillar abscess
  e. Sleep apnea

Options:
  a. Ascending Pharyngeal artery
✓ b. Branch of facial artery
  c. Descending Pharyngeal artery branch
  d. Lingual artery branch
  e. Paratonsillar vein

Explanation:
The tonsillar branch of the facial artery is the principal arterial supply to the tonsil and the most significant source of bleeding.

Question 513 (KMC - 2024)

Question:
A 50-year-old man was brought to the emergency room complaining of difficulty in breathing for the last three days. He is an uncontrolled diabetic, and also giving history of dental manipulation by dentist. On examination the tongue is protruded with sub-lingual swelling. What is the most appropriate clinical diagnosis?

Options:
✓ a. Ludwig’s Angina
  b. Parapharyngeal abscess
  c. Retropharyngeal abscess
  d. Submandibular duct stone
  e. Submandibular abscess

Explanation:
Ludwig's Angina is a rapidly spreading cellulitis of the submandibular/sublingual space, often of dental origin, pushing the tongue upwards and backwards.

Question:

Options:
  a. Endotracheal Intubation
  b. High Tracheostomy
  c. Low Tracheostomy
  d. Percutaneous Tracheostomy
✓ e. Tracheostomy between ring two and three of trachea

Explanation:
A standard tracheostomy (between 2nd and 3rd tracheal rings) is the preferred method to secure the airway in laryngeal carcinoma presenting with obstruction.

  a. Stage 1
✓ b. Stage 1a
  c. Stage 1b
  d. Stage 2a
  e. Stage 2b

Explanation:
A tumor limited to one vocal cord with normal mobility is classified as T1a (often referred to as Stage 1a in the context of T-stage grouping options).

  d. Total laryngectomy
  e. Sub epithelial Cordectomy

Explanation:

  c. Re-Bronchoscopy
  d. Tracheostomy
  e. Ventilator support

Options:
  a. Choanal atresia
  b. Epiglottitis
✓ c. Laryngomalacia
  d. Laryngotracheobronchitis
  e. Vocal cord Web

Explanation:

  b. Erythroplakia
  c. Leukoplakia
  d. Submucosal fibrosis
  e. Squamous cell carcinoma

Explanation:

  e. Ultrasound b mode tongue

Question 532 (KMC - 2024)

Question:

Patient is giving history of nasal obstruction and post-nasal drip on examination. There is mucopus in both nostrils. What specific investigation would you like to order immediately?

Options:
  a. CSF analysis
✓ b. CT Brain with contrast
  c. CT Brain without contrast
  d. MRI Brain
  e. PET scan

Explanation:
The symptoms suggest an intracranial complication of sinusitis (e.g., meningitis or abscess). A contrast-enhanced CT brain is urgent to visualize abscesses or meningeal enhancement.

Question 533 (KMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 53-year-old man presented to you with pain and redness in his left eye for the last 05 days. On examination, he has corneal ulceration involving inferior 1/3 of the cornea. He also has a left sided facial palsy. What is your diagnosis?

Options:
✓ Exposure keratopathy
  Filamentary keratopathy
  Infectious crystalline keratopathy
  Neurotrophic keratopathy
  Thygeson superficial punctate keratitis

Explanation:
Facial nerve palsy prevents complete closure of the eyelid (a condition called lagophthalmos). This leads to inadequate lubrication and drying of the cornea, especially the inferior third, resulting in epithelial breakdown and ulceration known as exposure keratopathy.

Question 534 (KMC - 2023)
================================================================================

Question:
A 7-years old boy developed pain and swelling of right orbital region for 3 days. He has a history of sinusitis for 1 week. On examination, he has right proptosis with reduced ocular motility and conjunctival chemosis. His vision is 6/36 in right eye. What is the most appropriate treatment in this patient?

Options:
✓ Systemic antibiotics
  Systemic steroids
  Topical antibiotics
  Topical cycloplegics
  Topical steroids

Explanation:
This patient has orbital cellulitis, a serious infection of the tissues behind the orbital septum, which is a common complication of sinusitis. It is a medical emergency that requires immediate hospitalization and treatment with broad-spectrum intravenous (systemic) antibiotics to prevent vision loss and intracranial spread.

================================================================================
Question 535 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#30. A patient of 45 years old presented with facial palsy. The epiphora (tearing) in this patient was due to:

Options:
  Ectropion
✓ Lacrimal pump failure
  Entropion

Explanation:
In facial nerve palsy, the orbicularis oculi muscle is weakened. This causes both ectropion (lid sagging) and failure of the lacrimal pump mechanism, which relies on normal blinking to move tears into the drainage system. Lacrimal pump failure is the primary reason for epiphora.

================================================================================
Question 536 (KMC - 2024)
================================================================================

Question:
Q#39. An infant presented with hyperpurulent, sticky discharge from both eyes and extreme congestion of the conjunctiva. The provisional diagnosis is ophthalmia neonatorum. Which organism causes this severe presentation?

Options:
  Diphtheria
  E. coli
✓ Gonococcus

Explanation:
Ophthalmia neonatorum caused by Neisseria gonorrhoeae (Gonococcus) is characterized by a hyperacute onset (within 2-5 days of birth) with severe, copious, purulent discharge, and marked conjunctival inflammation. It is an ophthalmologic emergency.

Question 537 (NMC - 2023)

Question:
A 20-year-old female presented with unilateral nasal obstruction for the last 6 months. On examination, a fleshy mass can be seen in the right nostril and also a mass can be seen hanging down in the oropharynx. What is the most likely diagnosis?

Options:
  a. Ethmoidal polyp
✓ b. Antrochoanal polyp
  c. Inverted papilloma
  d. Squamous cell carcinoma

Explanation:
Antrochoanal polyps arise from the maxillary antrum and grow backwards into the choana and nasopharynx, often presenting as a mass visible in the oropharynx.

Question 539 (NMC - 2023)

  b. Acute catarrhal tonsillitis

Question 540 (NMC - 2023)

Question:
A 35-year-old female presented with difficulty in swallowing for the last one month. She looks pale and has brittle nails; she has no history of weight loss. The peripheral smear shows microcytic hypochromic anemia. What is the most likely diagnosis?

Options:
  a. Thalassemia
✓ b. Plummer-Vinson syndrome
  c. Fanconi anemia
  d. Hypopharyngeal carcinoma

Explanation:
Plummer-Vinson syndrome (Paterson-Brown-Kelly syndrome) is characterized by the triad of dysphagia, iron deficiency anemia (microcytic hypochromic), and esophageal webs.

  d. 30 days

  d. Horgan’s operation

Options:
  a. Very painful
  b. Causes metastatic emboli
✓ c. Erodes bone
  d. Rapidly progressive condition

Explanation:
Cholesteatoma is a destructive lesion that produces enzymes (collagenases) capable of eroding surrounding bone, leading to complications.

  d. Facial nerve section and grafting

  b. Brain abscess
  c. Meningitis
✓ d. Lateral sinus thrombosis
  e. Extra dural abscess

Question 550 (NMC - 2023)

Question:
In malignant otitis externa, the causative organism is:

Options:
  a. Streptococcus aureus
  b. H. influenza
